## Chapter: Chapter 6: Turbulence in the Ocean

### Introduction

Turbulence, a complex and chaotic phenomenon, is ubiquitous in the natural world. It is a key player in the ocean, influencing a wide range of processes from the mixing of heat and salt to the transport of nutrients and pollutants. This chapter, "Turbulence in the Ocean," aims to delve into the intricate world of oceanic turbulence, exploring its causes, characteristics, and consequences.

Oceanic turbulence is a fascinating subject, not only because of its inherent complexity but also due to its profound impact on the global climate system. The ocean, covering about 70% of the Earth's surface, plays a crucial role in regulating the planet's climate. Turbulence in the ocean contributes to the vertical mixing of heat, salt, and other properties, thereby affecting the ocean's stratification and circulation patterns. These, in turn, influence the global climate system.

Understanding oceanic turbulence is not a straightforward task. It involves a multitude of scales, from the tiny Kolmogorov microscales to the vast ocean basin scales. The turbulence is driven by various forces, including wind stress at the surface, tidal forces, and buoyancy forces due to temperature and salinity gradients. The interaction of these forces leads to a rich variety of turbulent flows, each with its unique characteristics and implications.

This chapter will provide a comprehensive overview of oceanic turbulence. We will start by discussing the fundamental concepts and theories of turbulence, including the famous Kolmogorov's theory. We will then delve into the various sources of turbulence in the ocean, exploring how they interact to generate the observed turbulent flows. We will also discuss the methods used to measure and model oceanic turbulence, highlighting the challenges and recent advances in this field. Finally, we will examine the role of turbulence in the ocean's heat and salt transport, and its implications for the global climate system.

In this journey through the world of oceanic turbulence, we hope to provide a deeper understanding of this complex phenomenon and its far-reaching impacts. Whether you are a student, a researcher, or simply someone interested in the mysteries of the ocean, we believe this chapter will offer valuable insights and stimulate further exploration into this fascinating subject.

### Section: 6.1 Oceanic Turbulence:

Oceanic turbulence is a complex and multifaceted phenomenon that plays a crucial role in the ocean's dynamics and its interaction with the atmosphere. It is a key driver of the ocean's mixing processes, influencing the distribution of heat, salt, nutrients, and pollutants. Understanding the generation and characteristics of oceanic turbulence is therefore essential for a comprehensive understanding of the ocean's role in the Earth's climate system.

#### 6.1a Oceanic Turbulence Generation

The generation of oceanic turbulence is a result of the interaction of various forces and processes. These include wind stress at the ocean surface, tidal forces, and buoyancy forces due to temperature and salinity gradients. 

Wind stress at the ocean surface is a primary source of turbulence. The friction between the wind and the ocean surface generates turbulent eddies, which can penetrate to significant depths depending on the wind's strength and duration. This process is particularly important in the upper ocean, where it contributes to the vertical mixing of heat and momentum.

Tidal forces also play a crucial role in generating turbulence. The gravitational pull of the moon and the sun causes the ocean's tides, which can generate turbulence through the interaction with the ocean's bottom topography and the Coriolis effect. This tidal mixing is particularly significant in shallow coastal areas and around seamounts and ridges.

Buoyancy forces due to temperature and salinity gradients are another important source of turbulence. Differences in temperature and salinity create density gradients in the ocean, leading to buoyancy-driven flows. When these flows become unstable, they can generate turbulence. This process, known as convective instability, is a key driver of turbulence in the ocean's interior.

In addition to these primary sources, other processes such as internal waves, shear instabilities, and the breaking of surface waves can also generate turbulence. The interaction of these various sources leads to a rich variety of turbulent flows, each with its unique characteristics and implications.

Understanding the generation of oceanic turbulence is a complex task due to the multitude of scales involved, from the tiny Kolmogorov microscales to the vast ocean basin scales. It requires a combination of observational data, theoretical models, and numerical simulations. Despite the challenges, significant progress has been made in recent years, enhancing our understanding of this fascinating and important phenomenon.

#### 6.1b Oceanic Turbulence Dissipation

The dissipation of oceanic turbulence is a critical process that contributes to the overall energy balance of the ocean. It is the mechanism by which the kinetic energy of turbulent motions is converted into heat, thereby increasing the ocean's internal energy and contributing to the ocean's heat budget.

The primary mechanism for the dissipation of turbulence in the ocean is through the action of molecular viscosity. As turbulent eddies cascade from larger to smaller scales, they eventually reach a scale at which molecular viscosity becomes significant. At these scales, the kinetic energy of the turbulent motion is converted into heat through viscous dissipation. This process is often referred to as the "energy cascade" in turbulence.

The rate of energy dissipation due to turbulence, often denoted by $\epsilon$, is a key parameter in the study of oceanic turbulence. It is typically estimated from measurements of turbulent velocity and temperature fluctuations, using the relation:

$$
\epsilon = \nu \left( \frac{\partial u}{\partial z} \right)^2
$$

where $\nu$ is the molecular viscosity, $u$ is the velocity, and $z$ is the vertical coordinate. This equation is derived from the viscous dissipation term in the energy equation for turbulent flows.

The dissipation of turbulence also plays a crucial role in the subgridscale parameterization schemes used in ocean general circulation models. These schemes aim to represent the effects of unresolved turbulent motions on the resolved scales of motion. The dissipation of turbulence is one of the key processes that need to be parameterized in these schemes, as it affects the energy balance and mixing processes in the model.

In addition to molecular viscosity, other processes can also contribute to the dissipation of turbulence in the ocean. These include wave breaking, stratification, and interactions with the ocean bottom topography. However, these processes are often more difficult to quantify and are typically represented through empirical parameterizations in ocean models.

In conclusion, the dissipation of oceanic turbulence is a complex process that plays a crucial role in the ocean's dynamics and energy balance. Understanding this process is essential for accurately modeling the ocean's circulation and its role in the Earth's climate system.

#### 6.1c Oceanic Turbulence Modeling

Oceanic turbulence modeling is a crucial aspect of understanding and predicting the behavior of the ocean. It involves the use of mathematical models to represent the complex, chaotic motions of the ocean's waters. These models are essential tools in the study of oceanic turbulence, as they allow us to simulate and analyze turbulent flows that would be otherwise impossible to study directly.

One of the key challenges in oceanic turbulence modeling is the so-called "closure problem". This problem arises from the fact that the equations of motion for turbulent flows, known as the Reynolds-averaged Navier-Stokes (RANS) equations, contain more unknowns than equations. This is due to the fact that turbulence involves a wide range of scales of motion, from large-scale currents down to small-scale eddies, and the RANS equations cannot fully capture this multiscale nature.

To overcome the closure problem, oceanic turbulence models often employ subgridscale (SGS) parameterization schemes. These schemes aim to represent the effects of the unresolved, small-scale turbulent motions on the resolved, large-scale motions. The SGS parameterization schemes are based on various assumptions and approximations, and they are a subject of ongoing research and development.

The SGS parameterization schemes can be broadly classified into two categories: lateral and vertical. The lateral schemes aim to represent the effects of horizontal turbulent motions, while the vertical schemes focus on the vertical motions. These schemes often employ filters and higher-order operators to remove small-scale noise that is numerically necessary.

In addition to the general SGS parameterization schemes, there are also special dynamical parameterizations for certain processes, such as topographic stress, eddy thickness diffusion, and convection. These special parameterizations are designed to account for the specific effects of these processes on the turbulent flows.

The surface mixed layer (SML) is a particularly important region in the ocean, as it plays a crucial role in air-sea exchange. Therefore, the SML has historically received special attention in the development of SGS parameterization schemes. There are now many different schemes available for representing the effects of turbulence in the SML.

The development and validation of oceanic turbulence models is a complex task that requires a combination of theoretical analysis, numerical simulations, and observational data. Despite the challenges, these models are essential tools in the study of oceanic turbulence, and they play a crucial role in our ability to understand and predict the behavior of the ocean.

#### 6.1d Oceanic Turbulence Observations

Observations of oceanic turbulence are critical for validating and refining the mathematical models and parameterization schemes discussed in the previous section. These observations can be obtained through a variety of methods, including in-situ measurements, remote sensing, and the use of ocean general circulation models.

In-situ measurements of oceanic turbulence are often obtained through the deployment of oceanographic instruments such as current meters, thermistors, and conductivity-temperature-depth (CTD) sensors. These instruments can provide valuable data on the velocity, temperature, and salinity of the ocean, which are key parameters in the study of oceanic turbulence. However, in-situ measurements are typically limited in spatial and temporal coverage due to logistical and financial constraints.

Remote sensing techniques, such as satellite altimetry and synthetic aperture radar (SAR), offer a way to obtain large-scale, continuous observations of the ocean surface. These techniques can provide information on sea surface height, wave height, and surface roughness, which can be used to infer the presence and intensity of turbulence. However, remote sensing techniques can only observe the surface layer of the ocean, and they are subject to various sources of error and uncertainty.

Ocean general circulation models (OGCMs), such as those used in the RAPID program, are powerful tools for studying oceanic turbulence. These models simulate the large-scale circulation of the ocean, taking into account the effects of wind stress, heat flux, and freshwater flux, as well as the influence of the Earth's rotation and topography. By incorporating subgridscale parameterization schemes, OGCMs can also represent the effects of small-scale turbulent motions on the large-scale circulation. However, the accuracy of OGCMs is dependent on the quality of the input data and the validity of the underlying assumptions and parameterizations.

Despite these challenges, observations of oceanic turbulence have led to significant advances in our understanding of the ocean. They have revealed the complex, multiscale nature of oceanic turbulence, and they have provided valuable insights into the role of turbulence in the transport of heat, salt, and momentum in the ocean. These observations have also highlighted the need for continued research and development in the field of oceanic turbulence modeling and parameterization.

### Section: 6.2 Oceanic Eddies:

Oceanic eddies are a significant component of oceanic turbulence. These are large rotating structures that are formed by the instability of ocean currents. They play a crucial role in the transport of heat, salt, nutrients, and other properties across the ocean. 

#### 6.2a Oceanic Eddy Generation

The generation of oceanic eddies is primarily driven by the instability of large-scale ocean currents. This instability can be caused by a variety of factors, including changes in wind stress, temperature gradients, and the rotation of the Earth. 

One of the main mechanisms for eddy generation is baroclinic instability. This occurs when there is a vertical gradient in temperature and salinity, leading to a density gradient. The density gradient can destabilize the flow, causing it to meander and eventually break down into eddies. This process is particularly prevalent in the Antarctic Circumpolar Current (ACC), where strong wind stress and temperature gradients are present.

Another important mechanism for eddy generation is barotropic instability. This occurs when there is a horizontal gradient in velocity, such as in the Gulf Stream. The velocity gradient can cause the flow to become unstable and form eddies. 

Eddies can also be generated by topographic features such as seamounts and continental slopes. These features can deflect the flow and cause it to become unstable, leading to the formation of eddies.

#### 6.2b Eddy Saturation and Eddy Compensation

Eddy saturation and eddy compensation are two important concepts in the study of oceanic eddies. Eddy saturation refers to the insensitivity of the mean current to the accelerating wind forcing, while eddy compensation refers to the near independence of the Meridional Overturning Circulation (MOC) to the increase of wind stress.

In the ACC, it is currently assumed that the current is fully eddy saturated, but only partially eddy compensated. This means that the transport in the ACC is insensitive to changes in wind stress, but the MOC is not completely independent of wind stress. The degree to which there is eddy compensation in the Southern Ocean is currently unknown.

#### 6.2c Models and Sensitivity

Eddy permitting and eddy resolving models are used to examine the effect of eddy saturation and eddy compensation in the ACC. These models are of great importance in understanding the dynamics of oceanic eddies. However, the resolution of these models is a critical factor, as ocean observations do not have a high enough resolution to fully estimate the degree of eddy saturation and eddy compensation.

In these models, it has been found that the MOC response is more sensitive to model resolution than the ACC transport. This suggests that the dynamics of the MOC are more complex and require a higher resolution to accurately capture. Furthermore, these models have shown that southward eddy transport in combination with enhanced westerlies results in an increase in eddy kinetic energy.

is insensitive to changes in wind stress, but the MOC is not completely independent of these changes. 

#### 6.2c Oceanic Eddy Dynamics

Understanding the dynamics of oceanic eddies is crucial for predicting their behavior and their impact on the larger oceanic and atmospheric systems. The dynamics of oceanic eddies are governed by a complex interplay of forces, including the Coriolis effect, pressure gradients, and frictional forces.

The Coriolis effect, caused by the rotation of the Earth, imparts a rotational motion to the eddies. In the Northern Hemisphere, this causes the eddies to rotate in a clockwise direction, while in the Southern Hemisphere, they rotate counterclockwise. This rotation is a key factor in determining the direction of heat and nutrient transport by the eddies.

Pressure gradients, caused by differences in water density, also play a crucial role in eddy dynamics. These gradients can drive the flow of water within the eddy, leading to the formation of a circular current. The strength and direction of this current can influence the transport of heat and nutrients by the eddy.

Frictional forces, caused by the interaction of the eddy with the surrounding water and the ocean floor, can also influence eddy dynamics. These forces can cause the eddy to lose energy and eventually dissipate. The rate of dissipation can depend on a variety of factors, including the size of the eddy, the depth of the water, and the roughness of the ocean floor.

Eddy dynamics can also be influenced by external factors such as wind stress and topography. For example, wind stress can impart energy to the eddy, causing it to grow in size and strength. Similarly, topographic features such as seamounts and continental slopes can deflect the flow of water, leading to the formation of eddies.

In conclusion, the dynamics of oceanic eddies are governed by a complex interplay of forces and factors. Understanding these dynamics is crucial for predicting the behavior of eddies and their impact on the larger oceanic and atmospheric systems.

#### 6.2c Oceanic Eddy Dissipation

The dissipation of oceanic eddies is a crucial aspect of their lifecycle and has significant implications for the transport of heat and nutrients in the ocean. The process of eddy dissipation involves the loss of energy from the eddy, which can occur through several mechanisms.

One of the primary mechanisms of eddy dissipation is through frictional forces. As an eddy moves through the water, it experiences resistance from the surrounding water and the ocean floor. This resistance, or drag, causes the eddy to lose energy and gradually dissipate. The rate of dissipation can depend on several factors, including the size of the eddy, the depth of the water, and the roughness of the ocean floor.

Another mechanism of eddy dissipation is through the process of wave radiation. As an eddy moves through the water, it can generate waves that radiate away from the eddy. These waves carry energy away from the eddy, leading to its dissipation. The rate of wave radiation can depend on the speed of the eddy, the stratification of the water, and the presence of other eddies or currents.

Eddy dissipation can also occur through the process of turbulent mixing. This involves the transfer of energy from the eddy to the surrounding water through turbulent fluctuations. The rate of turbulent mixing can depend on the intensity of the eddy, the stability of the water, and the presence of other eddies or currents.

The dissipation of oceanic eddies is a complex process that involves a delicate balance of forces. Understanding this process is crucial for predicting the behavior of eddies and their impact on the larger oceanic and atmospheric systems. Future research in this area could provide valuable insights into the role of eddies in the ocean's heat and nutrient transport, as well as their potential effects on climate change. 

In the context of eddy compensation, the dissipation of eddies can influence the balance between the Ekman transport and the eddy-induced transport. This can have implications for the sensitivity of the ACC and MOC to changes in wind stress. Further research is needed to fully understand the role of eddy dissipation in eddy compensation and its potential impacts on ocean circulation and climate.

#### 6.2d Oceanic Eddy Observations

Observing and measuring oceanic eddies is a challenging task due to their transient nature and the vastness of the ocean. However, advancements in technology and the development of sophisticated models have made it possible to gain valuable insights into the behavior of these eddies.

One of the primary methods of observing oceanic eddies is through satellite altimetry. This technique involves the use of radar signals from satellites to measure the sea surface height. Since eddies cause variations in the sea surface height, they can be detected and tracked using this method. Satellite altimetry has been instrumental in identifying and studying large-scale eddies in the ocean.

In addition to satellite altimetry, in-situ measurements are also used to observe oceanic eddies. These measurements are typically obtained using instruments such as Argo floats, which are autonomous devices that drift with the ocean currents and measure temperature and salinity at different depths. These data can be used to infer the presence of eddies and study their characteristics.

Numerical models also play a crucial role in the study of oceanic eddies. Eddy permitting and eddy resolving models, as mentioned in the context, are used to examine the effect of eddy saturation and eddy compensation in the ACC. These models can simulate the formation, evolution, and dissipation of eddies, providing valuable insights into their dynamics.

However, it is important to note that the resolution of these models and observations is a critical factor in accurately capturing the behavior of eddies. Ocean observations do not have a high enough resolution to fully estimate the degree of eddy saturation and eddy compensation. Therefore, there is a need for more high-resolution observations and models to improve our understanding of oceanic eddies.

In the context of eddy compensation, observations of oceanic eddies can provide valuable information about the balance between the Ekman transport and the eddy-induced transport. By studying the behavior of eddies, we can gain insights into the mechanisms of eddy compensation and their implications for the oceanic and atmospheric systems.

In conclusion, observing and understanding oceanic eddies is a complex task that requires the use of advanced technology and sophisticated models. Despite the challenges, these efforts are crucial for improving our understanding of the ocean's dynamics and its role in the global climate system. Future research in this area has the potential to provide valuable insights into the behavior of oceanic eddies and their impact on the larger oceanic and atmospheric systems.

### Section: 6.3 Oceanic Boundary Layers:

The oceanic boundary layer is a critical component of the ocean's structure, playing a significant role in the exchange of heat, momentum, and mass between the ocean and the atmosphere. This layer is characterized by turbulence, which is driven by various factors such as wind stress, buoyancy fluxes, and wave breaking.

#### 6.3a Oceanic Boundary Layer Structure

The oceanic boundary layer can be divided into three main layers: the surface mixed layer, the pycnocline (or thermocline), and the deep ocean. 

The surface mixed layer is the uppermost layer of the ocean, typically extending to a depth of about 100 to 200 meters. This layer is directly influenced by atmospheric conditions and is characterized by nearly uniform temperature and salinity. The turbulence in this layer is primarily driven by wind stress and heat fluxes, leading to vertical mixing of properties.

Below the surface mixed layer is the pycnocline, a region of rapid change in density with depth, primarily due to changes in temperature (thermocline) and salinity (halocline). The turbulence in this layer is weaker than in the surface mixed layer, but it plays a crucial role in the vertical exchange of heat and other properties between the surface and the deep ocean.

The deep ocean, which extends from the base of the pycnocline to the ocean floor, is characterized by relatively stable stratification and weak turbulence. However, in certain regions such as the Labrador Sea and the Greenland Sea, deep convection can occur, leading to intense turbulence and mixing in the deep ocean.

Understanding the structure and dynamics of the oceanic boundary layer is crucial for predicting the ocean's response to atmospheric forcing and its role in climate variability. For instance, the depth of the surface mixed layer can influence the ocean's heat content and its ability to absorb atmospheric CO$_2$. Similarly, the strength of turbulence in the pycnocline can affect the vertical redistribution of heat and nutrients in the ocean.

In the following sections, we will delve deeper into the dynamics of each of these layers and the factors that influence their turbulence characteristics.

#### 6.3b Oceanic Boundary Layer Dynamics

The dynamics of the oceanic boundary layer are complex and multifaceted, involving a range of physical processes and interactions. These dynamics are primarily driven by the balance between the forces of turbulence and the stabilizing effects of stratification.

Turbulence in the oceanic boundary layer is generated by several mechanisms. Wind stress at the ocean surface is a primary source, inducing shear and leading to turbulent mixing in the surface mixed layer. Wave breaking also contributes to turbulence generation, particularly in stormy conditions. Additionally, buoyancy fluxes due to heat and freshwater inputs can drive turbulence, with positive buoyancy fluxes leading to convective instability and mixing.

The effects of turbulence are counteracted by the stabilizing influence of stratification, which acts to suppress vertical mixing. Stratification in the ocean is primarily due to variations in temperature and salinity with depth, leading to density gradients that resist vertical motion. The balance between turbulence and stratification determines the structure and dynamics of the oceanic boundary layer.

In the surface mixed layer, turbulence typically dominates over stratification, leading to a well-mixed layer with nearly uniform properties. The depth of the surface mixed layer can vary significantly, depending on the balance between wind-driven mixing and buoyancy fluxes. For instance, strong winds can deepen the mixed layer, while positive buoyancy fluxes due to surface warming can lead to its shoaling.

Below the surface mixed layer, in the pycnocline, stratification becomes stronger and turbulence weaker. However, turbulence in the pycnocline is crucial for the vertical exchange of heat, salt, and other properties between the surface and the deep ocean. This exchange can have significant implications for the ocean's role in climate, as it affects the ocean's heat content and carbon storage capacity.

In the deep ocean, stratification is typically strong and turbulence weak, leading to a relatively stable environment. However, in certain regions and under specific conditions, deep convection can occur, leading to intense turbulence and mixing in the deep ocean. Understanding these dynamics is crucial for predicting the ocean's response to changes in atmospheric forcing and its role in climate variability.

The dynamics of the oceanic boundary layer are also influenced by subgrid-scale processes, which need to be parameterized in ocean models. These processes include molecular friction and diffusion, which, while negligible at large scales, are essential at smaller scales. The parameterization of these processes is a challenging aspect of ocean modeling, requiring a careful balance between physical realism and computational efficiency. 

In conclusion, the dynamics of the oceanic boundary layer are complex and involve a range of physical processes and scales. Understanding these dynamics is crucial for predicting the ocean's response to atmospheric forcing and its role in climate variability.

```
#### 6.3c Oceanic Boundary Layer Mixing

The mixing within the oceanic boundary layer is a critical process that influences the distribution of heat, salt, and other properties in the ocean. This mixing is primarily driven by turbulence, which can be generated by several mechanisms, including wind stress, wave breaking, and buoyancy fluxes.

The role of wind stress in generating turbulence and mixing in the oceanic boundary layer has been well established. Wind stress induces shear at the ocean surface, leading to the generation of turbulent eddies that mix the surface waters. This process is particularly effective in the surface mixed layer, where turbulence typically dominates over stratification, leading to a well-mixed layer with nearly uniform properties.

Wave breaking is another important mechanism for turbulence generation and mixing in the oceanic boundary layer. When waves break, they generate turbulence that can mix the surface waters and contribute to the deepening of the surface mixed layer. This process is particularly significant in stormy conditions, where wave breaking can be intense.

Buoyancy fluxes due to heat and freshwater inputs can also drive turbulence and mixing in the oceanic boundary layer. Positive buoyancy fluxes, such as those due to surface warming or freshwater inputs, can lead to convective instability and mixing. This process can be particularly significant in regions of deep convection, such as the Labrador Sea, where the weak density stratification allows for intense wintertime convection and mixing (Kanzow et al., 2007; Cunningham et al., 2007).

The mixing in the oceanic boundary layer can have significant implications for the ocean's role in climate. For instance, the vertical exchange of heat and salt between the surface and the deep ocean, driven by turbulence and mixing in the pycnocline, can affect the ocean's heat content and carbon storage capacity. This exchange can also influence the strength of the Meridional Overturning Circulation (MOC), which plays a crucial role in the global climate system (Johns et al., 2009).

In conclusion, the mixing in the oceanic boundary layer, driven by turbulence generated by wind stress, wave breaking, and buoyancy fluxes, plays a crucial role in the distribution of heat, salt, and other properties in the ocean, and in the ocean's role in the global climate system.
```

#### 6.3d Oceanic Boundary Layer Observations

Observations of the oceanic boundary layer are crucial for understanding the dynamics of turbulence and mixing in the ocean. These observations can be obtained through various methods, including in-situ measurements, remote sensing, and numerical modeling.

In-situ measurements provide direct observations of the oceanic boundary layer. For instance, the RAPID program has been monitoring the ocean circulation since 2004, providing valuable data on the oceanic boundary layer (Proudman Oceanographic Laboratory). These measurements can provide insights into the spatial and temporal variability of turbulence and mixing in the oceanic boundary layer.

Remote sensing techniques, such as satellite observations, can provide large-scale, continuous observations of the oceanic boundary layer. These observations can be used to infer the distribution of turbulence and mixing in the oceanic boundary layer, although they are typically less direct and more uncertain than in-situ measurements.

Numerical modeling can also provide valuable insights into the dynamics of the oceanic boundary layer. These models can simulate the processes that generate turbulence and mixing in the oceanic boundary layer, such as wind stress, wave breaking, and buoyancy fluxes. They can also simulate the effects of these processes on the distribution of heat, salt, and other properties in the ocean.

Observations of the oceanic boundary layer have revealed significant spatial and temporal variability in turbulence and mixing. For instance, deep convection is observed in the subpolar North Atlantic, the Weddell Sea in the southern hemisphere, and the northwestern Mediterranean (Holdsworth et al., 2015). These regions are characterized by weak density stratification, which allows for intense wintertime convection and mixing. The Labrador Sea, in particular, is one of the most extreme ocean convection sites in the world, with depths between 1000 and 2000 m observed each winter.

These observations highlight the importance of the oceanic boundary layer in the ocean's role in climate. The vertical exchange of heat and salt between the surface and the deep ocean, driven by turbulence and mixing in the oceanic boundary layer, can affect the ocean's heat content and carbon storage capacity. This exchange can also influence the strength of the Meridional Overturning Circulation, a key component of the global climate system.

### Section: 6.4 Oceanic Internal Waves:

Oceanic internal waves are a significant component of the ocean's turbulence. These waves occur within the ocean's interior, where they are driven by tidal forces, wind stress, and the interaction of ocean currents with the seafloor topography. They play a crucial role in the transport of heat, salt, and nutrients within the ocean, and they can significantly influence the ocean's response to atmospheric forcing.

#### 6.4a Oceanic Internal Wave Generation

The generation of oceanic internal waves is a complex process that involves several mechanisms. One of the primary mechanisms is the interaction of tidal currents with the seafloor topography. As tidal currents flow over irregularities in the seafloor, they can generate internal waves that propagate vertically into the ocean's interior. This process is particularly effective in generating internal waves in regions where the tidal currents are strong and the seafloor topography is complex, such as around seamounts and continental slopes.

Another important mechanism for the generation of oceanic internal waves is wind stress. When the wind blows over the ocean surface, it can generate surface waves that can interact with the ocean's stratification to generate internal waves. This process is most effective in regions where the wind is strong and the ocean's stratification is weak, such as in the upper ocean during periods of strong wind forcing.

The interaction of ocean currents with the seafloor topography can also generate internal waves. When an ocean current encounters a change in the seafloor topography, such as a ridge or a slope, it can generate internal waves that propagate away from the topography. This process is particularly effective in generating internal waves in regions where the ocean currents are strong and the seafloor topography is complex.

The generation of oceanic internal waves is also influenced by the ocean's stratification. The ocean is stratified in density, with denser water lying beneath less dense water. This stratification provides the restoring force that allows internal waves to propagate. When the stratification is strong, the speed of internal waves is high, and their wavelength is long. Conversely, when the stratification is weak, the speed of internal waves is low, and their wavelength is short.

The generation of oceanic internal waves is a crucial process in the ocean's turbulence. These waves can transport heat, salt, and nutrients vertically and horizontally within the ocean, and they can significantly influence the ocean's response to atmospheric forcing. Understanding the generation of oceanic internal waves is therefore essential for understanding the dynamics of turbulence in the ocean.

#### 6.4b Oceanic Internal Wave Propagation

The propagation of oceanic internal waves is a dynamic process that is influenced by a variety of factors. These waves travel through the ocean's interior, carrying energy and momentum from their source regions to distant locations. The propagation of these waves is primarily governed by the ocean's stratification and the Coriolis effect, which is due to the Earth's rotation.

The ocean's stratification, characterized by layers of water with different densities, plays a crucial role in the propagation of internal waves. The speed at which these waves travel, known as the phase speed, is determined by the buoyancy frequency, which is a measure of the stratification. The buoyancy frequency, $N$, is given by the formula:

$$
N = \sqrt{-\frac{g}{\rho_0}\frac{d\rho}{dz}}
$$

where $g$ is the acceleration due to gravity, $\rho_0$ is a reference density, $d\rho/dz$ is the vertical gradient of density, and $z$ is the vertical coordinate (positive upwards). The phase speed of an internal wave, $c$, is then given by:

$$
c = \frac{\omega}{k}
$$

where $\omega$ is the frequency of the wave and $k$ is the wavenumber. For a given frequency, the phase speed is inversely proportional to the wavenumber, which means that waves with longer wavelengths (smaller wavenumbers) travel faster than waves with shorter wavelengths (larger wavenumbers).

The Coriolis effect also plays a significant role in the propagation of oceanic internal waves. Due to the Earth's rotation, the motion of these waves is deflected to the right in the Northern Hemisphere and to the left in the Southern Hemisphere. This deflection, known as the Coriolis force, can cause the waves to propagate in a direction that is not parallel to the direction of the initial wave generation. The Coriolis parameter, $f$, is given by:

$$
f = 2\Omega \sin(\phi)
$$

where $\Omega$ is the Earth's rotation rate and $\phi$ is the latitude. The Coriolis effect becomes more pronounced as the latitude increases, which can lead to significant differences in the propagation of internal waves in different parts of the ocean.

In addition to these factors, the propagation of oceanic internal waves can also be influenced by the seafloor topography and the presence of ocean currents. When these waves encounter changes in the seafloor topography or strong ocean currents, they can be refracted, reflected, or even dissipated. These interactions can significantly alter the propagation paths of the waves and can lead to complex spatial patterns of wave energy distribution in the ocean's interior.

#### 6.4c Oceanic Internal Wave Breaking

The breaking of oceanic internal waves is a significant process that contributes to the mixing of the ocean's interior. This process is analogous to the breaking of surface waves, but it occurs beneath the ocean surface where the density stratification and the Coriolis effect play crucial roles.

When an internal wave propagates through the ocean, it can encounter regions where the stratification changes abruptly or where the wave's amplitude becomes so large that the wave overturns and breaks. This breaking process can lead to the formation of turbulent eddies, which enhance the mixing of heat, salt, and other properties within the ocean.

The breaking of an internal wave can be described by the wave's Richardson number, $Ri$, which is a dimensionless quantity that compares the wave's buoyancy frequency, $N$, to its vertical shear, $S$. The Richardson number is given by the formula:

$$
Ri = \frac{N^2}{S^2}
$$

where $S$ is the vertical shear of the wave, defined as the change in the wave's horizontal velocity with respect to depth. When the Richardson number falls below a critical value, typically around 0.25, the wave is unstable and can break.

The breaking of internal waves can also be influenced by the Coriolis effect. As discussed in the previous section, the Coriolis effect can deflect the motion of internal waves, causing them to propagate in a direction that is not parallel to the direction of the initial wave generation. This deflection can lead to the interaction of internal waves with the ocean's topography or with other waves, which can trigger wave breaking.

The breaking of internal waves is a key mechanism for the transfer of energy from the large scales of ocean circulation to the small scales of turbulence. This energy transfer is essential for maintaining the ocean's stratification and for driving the global overturning circulation, which plays a crucial role in the Earth's climate system.

#### 6.4d Oceanic Internal Wave Observations

Observations of oceanic internal waves are crucial for understanding their generation, propagation, and breaking, as well as their role in oceanic turbulence and mixing. These observations can be made using a variety of techniques, including in-situ measurements, remote sensing, and numerical modeling.

In-situ measurements of internal waves can be made using a variety of instruments, such as Conductivity-Temperature-Depth (CTD) profilers, Acoustic Doppler Current Profilers (ADCPs), and moored instruments. These instruments can measure properties such as temperature, salinity, and current velocity, which can be used to infer the presence of internal waves and their characteristics.

For example, the RAPID program, which has been monitoring the ocean circulation since 2004, has provided valuable data on the generation and propagation of internal waves in the North Atlantic. The weak density stratification of the Labrador Sea, observed each wintertime, is a prime location for the generation of internal waves due to its extreme ocean convection. The deep convection in the Labrador Sea is significantly affected by the North Atlantic Oscillation (NAO), with the oceanic heat loss from the Labrador Sea being higher during the positive phase of NAO, contributing to a deeper convection and potentially more intense internal waves.

Remote sensing techniques, such as satellite altimetry and Synthetic Aperture Radar (SAR), can also be used to observe internal waves. These techniques can provide a large-scale view of internal wave fields and can detect features such as wave fronts and wave-induced surface roughness.

Numerical modeling is another important tool for studying internal waves. Models can simulate the generation, propagation, and breaking of internal waves, and can be used to investigate the effects of various factors, such as stratification, rotation, and topography, on these processes. Models can also be used to study the effects of internal waves on larger-scale ocean circulation and mixing.

In conclusion, observations of oceanic internal waves are crucial for understanding their role in oceanic turbulence and mixing. These observations, combined with theoretical and numerical studies, can provide a comprehensive understanding of the dynamics of internal waves and their impact on the ocean and the Earth's climate system.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have delved into the complex and fascinating world of oceanic turbulence. We have explored the various factors that contribute to turbulence in the ocean, including wind, temperature, and salinity gradients, as well as the influence of the Earth's rotation. We have also examined the impact of turbulence on the distribution of heat, nutrients, and pollutants in the ocean, and its role in shaping marine ecosystems.

We have seen that turbulence in the ocean is a highly dynamic and multifaceted phenomenon, with a wide range of scales and intensities. It is not only a key driver of ocean circulation and climate, but also plays a crucial role in the life cycles of many marine organisms. Despite its importance, however, our understanding of oceanic turbulence remains incomplete, and much research is still needed to fully unravel its complexities.

In the next chapter, we will turn our attention to the atmosphere, where turbulence also plays a critical role. As we will see, the principles and concepts we have learned in this chapter will provide a solid foundation for our exploration of atmospheric turbulence.
