## Chapter 8: Turbulence Modeling

### Introduction

Turbulence, a complex and chaotic phenomenon, is a fundamental aspect of both the ocean and the atmosphere. It plays a crucial role in the transport and mixing of heat, momentum, and chemical species. Despite its significance, turbulence remains one of the most challenging aspects to model in the fields of oceanography and meteorology. This chapter, "Turbulence Modeling," aims to delve into the intricacies of this subject.

The chapter begins by providing a broad overview of turbulence, its characteristics, and its importance in the ocean and atmosphere. It then delves into the mathematical foundations of turbulence modeling, discussing the Navier-Stokes equations and the Reynolds-averaged Navier-Stokes (RANS) equations. The use of `$\tau_{ij}$` to represent the Reynolds stress tensor, a key component in these equations, will be explained in detail.

Following this, the chapter will explore various turbulence models, including the k-epsilon model, the k-omega model, and Large Eddy Simulation (LES). Each model's strengths, weaknesses, and appropriate applications will be discussed, providing readers with a comprehensive understanding of when and how to use each model.

The chapter will also discuss the challenges and limitations of turbulence modeling, such as the difficulty in accurately representing small-scale turbulent motions and the assumptions made in turbulence models. It will also touch upon the ongoing research and advancements in the field, highlighting the importance of continued study in this area.

In conclusion, this chapter aims to provide a comprehensive overview of turbulence modeling, its mathematical foundations, various models, and the challenges faced in the field. It is hoped that this will provide readers with a solid foundation for understanding and applying turbulence models in their own research and work.

### Section: 8.1 Turbulence Closure Models

Turbulence closure models are essential in turbulence modeling as they provide a means to close the system of equations used to describe turbulent flows. These models are necessary because the exact equations of motion, the Navier-Stokes equations, are too complex to solve directly for most practical applications. 

#### 8.1a Reynolds-Averaged Navier-Stokes Equations

The Reynolds-averaged Navier-Stokes (RANS) equations are a form of the Navier-Stokes equations that have been averaged over time to remove the small-scale turbulent fluctuations. This simplification allows for the modeling of turbulent flows at a reduced computational cost.

The RANS equations are derived by decomposing the flow variables into a time-averaged component and a fluctuating component. For instance, for an incompressible, viscous, Newtonian fluid, the continuity and momentum equations can be written as:

$$
\frac{\partial \overline{u_i}}{\partial x_i} = 0,
$$

and

$$
\frac{D \overline{u_i}}{Dt} = -\frac{\partial \left( \bar{p} + p' \right) }{\partial x_i} + \mu \left[ \frac{\partial^2 \left( \overline{u_i} + u_i' \right)}{\partial x_j \partial x_j} \right].
$$

After averaging, the continuity and momentum equations become:

$$
\frac{\partial \overline{u_i}}{\partial x_i} = 0,
$$

and

$$
\frac{D \overline{u_i}}{Dt} = -\frac{\partial \bar{p}}{\partial x_i} + \mu \frac{\partial^2 \overline{u_i}}{\partial x_j \partial x_j} - \frac{\partial \left( \rho \overline{u_i' u_j'} \right)}{\partial x_j},
$$

where $\rho \overline{u_i' u_j'}$ are the Reynolds stresses, which are collected with the viscous normal and shear stress terms, $\mu \frac{\partial \overline{u_i}}{\partial x_j}$.

The RANS equations are a set of partial differential equations that describe the motion of fluid in turbulent flow. However, they contain more unknowns than equations, which makes them impossible to solve without additional equations. These additional equations are provided by turbulence closure models, which are discussed in the following sections.

#### 8.1b Eddy Viscosity Models

Eddy viscosity models are a class of turbulence closure models that are based on the concept of turbulent or eddy viscosity. These models are used to approximate the Reynolds stresses in the RANS equations with the mean rate of deformation. 

One of the most widely used eddy viscosity models is the Spalart–Allmaras turbulence model. 

##### Spalart–Allmaras Turbulence Model

The Spalart–Allmaras model is a one-equation model that solves a transport equation for a modified turbulent viscosity, $\tilde{\nu}$. The turbulent eddy viscosity is given by:

$$
\frac{\partial \tilde{\nu}}{\partial t} + u_j \frac{\partial \tilde{\nu}}{\partial x_j} = C_{b1} [1 - f_{t2}] \tilde{S} \tilde{\nu} + \frac{1}{\sigma} \{ \nabla \cdot [(\nu + \tilde{\nu}) \nabla \tilde{\nu}] + C_{b2} | \nabla \tilde{\nu} |^2 \} - \left[C_{w1} f_w - \frac{C_{b1}}{\kappa^2} f_{t2}\right] \left( \frac{\tilde{\nu}}{d} \right)^2 + f_{t1} \Delta U^2
$$

where $f_{t1}$ and $f_{t2}$ are damping functions, $\tilde{S}$ is the vorticity magnitude, and $d$ is the distance from the closest surface. The constants in the model are determined from empirical data.

The Spalart–Allmaras model has been modified over the years to improve its performance. For instance, Spalart suggested using different values for the constants $C_{t3}$ and $C_{t4}$ to improve the model's safety. Other modifications include the DES (1999) and DDES (2006) models.

##### Model for Compressible Flows

The Spalart–Allmaras model can be adapted for compressible flows. In all cases, the turbulent dynamic viscosity is computed from $\mu_t = \rho \tilde{\nu}$, where $\rho$ is the local density. 

There are two main approaches to adapting the model for compressible flows. The first approach applies the original equation for $\tilde{\nu}$. In the second approach, the convective terms in the equation for $\tilde{\nu}$ are modified, but the right-hand side (RHS) remains the same as in the original model.

In the next section, we will discuss the Reynolds stress models, another class of turbulence closure models.

#### 8.1c Reynolds Stress Models

Reynolds Stress Models (RSM) are a class of turbulence closure models that solve transport equations for the Reynolds stresses, rather than using an eddy viscosity concept. These models are based on the Reynolds-averaged Navier-Stokes (RANS) equations, which are derived by decomposing the flow variables into a mean and a fluctuating component.

##### Reynolds Averaging of the Navier–Stokes Equations

For an incompressible, viscous, Newtonian fluid, the continuity and momentum equations—the incompressible Navier–Stokes equations—can be written (in a non-conservative form) as:

$$
\frac{\partial u_i}{\partial x_i} = 0,
$$

and

$$
\frac{D u_i}{D t} = -\frac{1}{\rho} \frac{\partial p}{\partial x_i} + \nu \frac{\partial^2 u_i}{\partial x_j \partial x_j},
$$

where $D/Dt$ is the Lagrangian derivative or the substantial derivative. 

Defining the flow variables with a time-averaged component and a fluctuating component, the continuity and momentum equations become:

$$
\frac{\partial \left( \overline{u_i} + u_i' \right)}{\partial x_i} = 0,
$$

and

$$
\frac{D \left( \overline{u_i} + u_i' \right)}{D t} = -\frac{1}{\rho} \frac{\partial \left( \bar{p} + p' \right) }{\partial x_i} + \nu \frac{\partial^2 \left( \overline{u_i} + u_i' \right)}{\partial x_j \partial x_j}.
$$

##### Advantages of RSM

The Reynolds Stress Models have several advantages over other turbulence models:

1) Unlike the k-ε model which uses an isotropic eddy viscosity, RSM solves all components of the turbulent transport. 

2) It is the most general of all turbulence models and works reasonably well for a large number of engineering flows.

3) It requires only the initial and/or boundary conditions to be supplied.

4) Since the production terms need not be modeled, it can selectively damp the stresses due to buoyancy, curvature effects etc.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the specifics of the Reynolds Stress Models, discussing their formulation and application in various fluid dynamics scenarios.

#### 8.1d Large Eddy Simulation

Large Eddy Simulation (LES) is a mathematical model used in computational fluid dynamics to simulate turbulence by filtering out the smaller scales of motion. This method is particularly useful in the study of turbulence in the ocean and atmosphere, where the scales of motion can vary greatly.

##### Numerical Methods for LES

LES involves the solution to the discrete filtered governing equations using computational fluid dynamics. LES resolves scales from the domain size $L$ down to the filter size $\Delta$, and as such a substantial portion of high wave number turbulent fluctuations must be resolved. This requires either high-order numerical schemes, or fine grid resolution if low-order numerical schemes are used.

The question of how fine a grid resolution $\Delta x$ is needed to resolve a filtered velocity field $\overline{u}(\boldsymbol{x})$ is addressed in Chapter 13 of Pope. Ghosal found that for low-order discretization schemes, such as those used in finite volume methods, the truncation error can be the same order as the subfilter scale contributions, unless the filter width $\Delta$ is considerably larger than the grid spacing $\Delta x$. While even-order schemes have truncation error, they are non-dissipative, and because subfilter scale models are dissipative, even-order schemes will not affect the subfilter scale model contributions as strongly as dissipative schemes.

##### Filter Implementation

The filtering operation in LES can be implicit or explicit. Implicit filtering recognizes that the subfilter scale model will dissipate in the same manner as many numerical schemes. In this way, the grid, or the numerical discretization scheme, can be assumed to be the LES low-pass filter. While this takes full advantage of the grid resolution, and eliminates the computational cost of calculating a subfilter scale model term, it is difficult to determine the shape of the LES filter that is associated with some numerical issues. Additionally, truncation error can also become an issue.

In explicit filtering, an LES filter is applied to the discretized Navier–Stokes equations, providing a well-defined filter shape and reducing the truncation error. However, this method requires additional computational resources to calculate the subfilter scale model term.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the specifics of the Large Eddy Simulation, discussing its formulation and application in the study of turbulence in the ocean and atmosphere.

### Section: 8.2 Direct Numerical Simulation:

Direct Numerical Simulation (DNS) is another method used in computational fluid dynamics to simulate turbulence. Unlike Large Eddy Simulation (LES), DNS resolves all scales of motion in a turbulent flow, from the smallest Kolmogorov scales to the largest energy-containing scales. This makes DNS a highly accurate method for studying turbulence, but it also requires substantial computational resources.

#### 8.2a Basics of Direct Numerical Simulation

Direct Numerical Simulation involves solving the Navier-Stokes equations, which describe the motion of fluid substances, without any modeling or approximation. This means that all the spatial scales of the turbulent motion are resolved, from the smallest scales where viscosity is important (the Kolmogorov microscales) to the largest scales where the energy is input into the system.

The main advantage of DNS is its accuracy. Since it does not rely on any modeling or approximation, DNS can provide a detailed and accurate picture of turbulence. This makes it a valuable tool for understanding the fundamental mechanisms of turbulence and for validating turbulence models.

However, the main drawback of DNS is its computational cost. The number of grid points required for DNS scales with the Reynolds number to the power of 9/4, according to the formula:

$$
N \propto Re^{9/4}
$$

where $N$ is the number of grid points and $Re$ is the Reynolds number. This means that even for moderate Reynolds numbers, DNS requires a large number of grid points, which in turn requires substantial computational resources.

#### Numerical Methods for DNS

The numerical methods used for DNS are similar to those used for LES. They involve discretizing the Navier-Stokes equations using methods such as finite difference, finite volume, or spectral methods. The choice of numerical method depends on the specific problem and the available computational resources.

One important aspect of DNS is the need for high-order numerical schemes. Since DNS resolves all scales of motion, it requires a high level of accuracy in the numerical discretization. Low-order schemes can introduce numerical errors that can contaminate the small-scale motions, which are crucial in DNS.

Another important aspect is the need for a fine grid resolution. The grid spacing $\Delta x$ must be small enough to resolve the smallest scales of motion. This requires a large number of grid points, which increases the computational cost of DNS.

Despite these challenges, DNS is a powerful tool for studying turbulence. It provides a detailed and accurate picture of turbulent flows, which can help to improve our understanding of turbulence and to develop more accurate turbulence models.

#### 8.2b Direct Numerical Simulation of Turbulent Flows

Direct Numerical Simulation (DNS) of turbulent flows involves the numerical solution of the full, unfiltered Navier-Stokes equations. This approach is distinct from other methods such as Large Eddy Simulation (LES) and Detached Eddy Simulation (DES), which involve some form of filtering or modeling to reduce the computational cost.

In DNS, the entire range of turbulent scales, from the smallest dissipative scales (Kolmogorov scales) to the largest energy-containing scales, are resolved. This requires a grid resolution that is fine enough to capture the smallest scales of turbulence, which can be extremely small, especially at high Reynolds numbers. The requirement for high grid resolution leads to a high computational cost for DNS, which scales with the Reynolds number as $N \propto Re^{9/4}$.

Despite its high computational cost, DNS provides a wealth of information about the turbulent flow. Since no modeling or approximation is involved, DNS can provide a detailed and accurate picture of the turbulence, including the complex interactions between different scales of motion. This makes DNS a valuable tool for studying the fundamental mechanisms of turbulence and for validating and improving turbulence models.

The numerical methods used for DNS are similar to those used for LES and DES. They involve discretizing the Navier-Stokes equations using methods such as finite difference, finite volume, or spectral methods. The choice of numerical method depends on the specific problem and the available computational resources.

One important aspect of DNS is the need for accurate and stable numerical methods. Since DNS involves solving the full Navier-Stokes equations without any filtering or modeling, any numerical errors can have a significant impact on the results. Therefore, high-order numerical methods are often used in DNS to ensure accuracy and stability.

In conclusion, while DNS is computationally expensive, it provides the most accurate and detailed information about turbulent flows. This makes it an invaluable tool for studying turbulence and for improving turbulence models. However, due to its high computational cost, DNS is typically used for fundamental studies and for cases with relatively low Reynolds numbers. For practical engineering applications with high Reynolds numbers, other methods such as LES and DES are typically used.

#### 8.2c Direct Numerical Simulation of Turbulent Boundary Layers

Direct Numerical Simulation (DNS) is also a powerful tool for studying turbulent boundary layers. Turbulent boundary layers are regions near a solid surface where the flow is turbulent and the velocity varies from zero at the wall to the free stream value away from the wall. These regions are of great interest in many practical applications, such as the design of aircraft and ships, where the behavior of the boundary layer can significantly affect the performance and efficiency of the design.

In DNS of turbulent boundary layers, the full Navier-Stokes equations are solved without any modeling or filtering, just like in DNS of turbulent flows. However, the boundary conditions at the wall introduce additional complexity. The no-slip condition at the wall, which states that the velocity of the fluid at the wall is zero, leads to a very high gradient of velocity near the wall. This requires a very fine grid resolution near the wall to accurately capture the velocity gradient, which adds to the computational cost of DNS.

Despite the additional complexity and computational cost, DNS of turbulent boundary layers provides valuable insights into the behavior of turbulence near walls. It can reveal the detailed structure of the boundary layer, including the formation and evolution of turbulent structures such as streaks and vortices. It can also provide detailed information about the distribution of wall shear stress, which is a key parameter in many practical applications.

The numerical methods used for DNS of turbulent boundary layers are similar to those used for DNS of turbulent flows. They involve discretizing the Navier-Stokes equations using methods such as finite difference, finite volume, or spectral methods. High-order numerical methods are often used to ensure accuracy and stability, especially near the wall where the velocity gradient is high.

In conclusion, while DNS of turbulent boundary layers is computationally expensive, it provides a wealth of detailed and accurate information about the behavior of turbulence near walls. This makes it a valuable tool for studying the fundamental mechanisms of wall turbulence and for improving turbulence models and design methods in practical applications.

#### 8.2d Direct Numerical Simulation of Turbulent Convection

Direct Numerical Simulation (DNS) is not only useful for studying turbulent boundary layers, but also for understanding turbulent convection. Turbulent convection is a phenomenon that occurs when heat is transferred through a fluid medium due to the fluid's motion. This process is crucial in many natural and industrial processes, such as the heat transfer in the ocean and atmosphere, the cooling of electronic devices, and the operation of heat exchangers.

In DNS of turbulent convection, the full Navier-Stokes equations, along with the energy equation, are solved without any modeling or filtering. The energy equation is crucial in this context as it accounts for the heat transfer due to both conduction and convection. The buoyancy force, which is the driving force for the fluid motion in turbulent convection, is included in the momentum equations. This force is proportional to the temperature difference between the fluid and its surroundings, which introduces an additional nonlinearity into the system of equations.

The numerical methods used for DNS of turbulent convection are similar to those used for DNS of turbulent flows and turbulent boundary layers. They involve discretizing the Navier-Stokes and energy equations using methods such as finite difference, finite volume, or spectral methods. High-order numerical methods are often used to ensure accuracy and stability. 

Temporal discretization is typically done using explicit methods, such as the second-order Adams-Bashforth scheme, as described in the MEMO model. However, certain terms, such as the nonhydrostatic pressure perturbation and the vertical turbulent diffusion, may be treated implicitly to ensure stability and to avoid an unacceptable reduction of the time increment.

The boundary conditions for DNS of turbulent convection can be more complex than those for DNS of turbulent flows or turbulent boundary layers. They may include not only no-slip conditions for the velocity at the walls, but also thermal boundary conditions, such as constant temperature or constant heat flux conditions at the walls. These thermal boundary conditions can significantly affect the behavior of the turbulent convection.

Despite the additional complexity and computational cost, DNS of turbulent convection provides valuable insights into the behavior of turbulence in the presence of heat transfer. It can reveal the detailed structure of the convective flow, including the formation and evolution of turbulent structures such as plumes and thermal boundary layers. It can also provide detailed information about the distribution of temperature and heat flux, which are key parameters in many practical applications.

In conclusion, while DNS of turbulent convection is computationally demanding and requires careful handling of the boundary conditions and the nonlinearity introduced by the buoyancy force, it is a powerful tool for studying the complex interactions between turbulence and heat transfer.

### Section: 8.3 Large Eddy Simulation:

#### 8.3a Basics of Large Eddy Simulation

Large Eddy Simulation (LES) is a mathematical model used in computational fluid dynamics to simulate turbulence by filtering out the smallest scales of the flow. This approach allows for the resolution of the large-scale structures that are most responsible for the energy transfer in a turbulent flow, while the effects of the smaller scales are modeled.

In LES, the governing equations are the filtered Navier-Stokes equations. The filtering operation separates the flow field into resolved large-scale motions and unresolved subfilter-scale motions. The large-scale motions are directly resolved, while the subfilter-scale motions are modeled. This separation is achieved by applying a filter to the Navier-Stokes equations, which results in a filtered velocity field $\overline{u}(\boldsymbol{x})$.

The filter size $\Delta$ is a crucial parameter in LES. It determines the scales of motion that are resolved and those that are modeled. The filter size should be large enough to include the energy-containing eddies but small enough to allow for a feasible computational cost. The choice of the filter size is often guided by the available computational resources and the specific details of the flow being simulated.

The numerical methods used in LES are similar to those used in Direct Numerical Simulation (DNS). They involve discretizing the filtered Navier-Stokes equations using methods such as finite difference, finite volume, or spectral methods. However, due to the filtering operation, the discretization in LES needs to be performed with care to ensure that the truncation error does not dominate the subfilter scale contributions.

The filtering operation in LES can be either implicit or explicit. In implicit filtering, the numerical discretization scheme is assumed to act as the LES filter. This approach eliminates the computational cost of calculating a subfilter scale model term but can lead to numerical issues. In explicit filtering, an LES filter is applied to the discretized Navier-Stokes equations, providing a well-defined filter shape and reducing the truncation error.

In the following sections, we will delve deeper into the numerical methods for LES, the implementation of the filtering operation, and the subfilter scale models used in LES.

#### 8.3b Large Eddy Simulation of Turbulent Flows

Large Eddy Simulation (LES) is a powerful tool for simulating turbulent flows. The key to its success lies in its ability to resolve the large-scale structures of turbulence, which are responsible for most of the energy transfer, while modeling the smaller scales. This section will delve into the specifics of how LES is used to simulate turbulent flows.

The first step in LES of turbulent flows is to discretize the filtered Navier-Stokes equations. This is done using numerical methods such as finite difference, finite volume, or spectral methods. The choice of numerical method depends on the specific details of the flow being simulated and the available computational resources. However, it is important to note that the discretization must be performed with care to ensure that the truncation error does not dominate the subfilter scale contributions. This is particularly crucial when low-order discretization schemes are used, as the truncation error can be of the same order as the subfilter scale contributions unless the filter width $\Delta$ is considerably larger than the grid spacing $\Delta x$.

The next step is the implementation of the filter. As mentioned earlier, the filtering operation in LES can be either implicit or explicit. In implicit filtering, the numerical discretization scheme is assumed to act as the LES filter. This approach eliminates the computational cost of calculating a subfilter scale model term but can lead to some numerical issues. For instance, it can be difficult to determine the shape of the LES filter, and truncation error can become an issue.

On the other hand, explicit filtering involves applying an LES filter to the discretized Navier-Stokes equations. This provides a well-defined filter shape and reduces the truncation error. However, it does come with the computational cost of calculating a subfilter scale model term.

In both cases, the filter size $\Delta$ is a critical parameter. It determines the scales of motion that are resolved and those that are modeled. The filter size should be large enough to include the energy-containing eddies but small enough to allow for a feasible computational cost.

In conclusion, LES is a powerful tool for simulating turbulent flows. However, its success depends on careful implementation of the numerical methods and the filter. With the right approach, LES can provide valuable insights into the complex dynamics of turbulence.

#### 8.3c Large Eddy Simulation of Turbulent Boundary Layers

Turbulent boundary layers present a unique challenge in the field of turbulence modeling. These layers, which form at the interface between a solid surface and a fluid, are characterized by a wide range of length and time scales. This makes them particularly difficult to simulate accurately using traditional turbulence models. However, Large Eddy Simulation (LES) has proven to be a powerful tool for simulating these complex flows.

In LES of turbulent boundary layers, the first step is to discretize the filtered Navier-Stokes equations, as in any LES simulation. The choice of numerical method for this discretization will depend on the specific details of the boundary layer flow being simulated. However, it is crucial to ensure that the truncation error does not dominate the subfilter scale contributions, particularly when low-order discretization schemes are used. This can be achieved by ensuring that the filter width $\Delta$ is considerably larger than the grid spacing $\Delta x$.

The next step is the implementation of the filter. In turbulent boundary layers, the choice between implicit and explicit filtering can have significant implications. Implicit filtering, where the numerical discretization scheme is assumed to act as the LES filter, can be advantageous due to its lower computational cost. However, it can be difficult to determine the shape of the LES filter in this case, and truncation error can become an issue.

Explicit filtering, on the other hand, involves applying an LES filter to the discretized Navier-Stokes equations. This provides a well-defined filter shape and reduces the truncation error. However, it does come with the computational cost of calculating a subfilter scale model term.

Regardless of the filtering method chosen, the filter size $\Delta$ is a critical parameter in LES of turbulent boundary layers. The filter size must be chosen carefully to ensure that the large-scale structures of turbulence, which are responsible for most of the energy transfer in the boundary layer, are adequately resolved.

In conclusion, LES is a powerful tool for simulating turbulent boundary layers. However, careful consideration must be given to the choice of numerical method, filtering implementation, and filter size to ensure accurate and efficient simulations.

#### 8.3d Large Eddy Simulation of Turbulent Convection

Turbulent convection, a phenomenon where turbulence and heat transfer are coupled, presents another unique challenge in turbulence modeling. This process is ubiquitous in both the atmosphere and the ocean, playing a crucial role in weather patterns, climate dynamics, and oceanic circulation. Large Eddy Simulation (LES) has been increasingly used to study turbulent convection due to its ability to resolve the large-scale structures that dominate the energy and heat transport.

In LES of turbulent convection, the filtered Navier-Stokes equations are coupled with an energy equation that accounts for the heat transfer. The discretization of these equations is a critical step, and the choice of numerical method will depend on the specific details of the convective flow being simulated. As with LES of turbulent boundary layers, it is essential to ensure that the truncation error does not dominate the subfilter scale contributions. This can be achieved by ensuring that the filter width $\Delta$ is considerably larger than the grid spacing $\Delta x$.

The implementation of the filter in LES of turbulent convection follows similar principles as in LES of turbulent boundary layers. Implicit filtering can be advantageous due to its lower computational cost, but it can be challenging to determine the shape of the LES filter, and truncation error can become an issue. Explicit filtering provides a well-defined filter shape and reduces the truncation error, but it does come with the computational cost of calculating a subfilter scale model term.

In turbulent convection, the filter size $\Delta$ is a critical parameter. It must be chosen carefully to ensure that the large-scale structures that dominate the energy and heat transport are adequately resolved. The subfilter scale model must also account for the effects of buoyancy, which can significantly influence the turbulence and heat transfer in convective flows.

In conclusion, LES is a powerful tool for studying turbulent convection, but it requires careful consideration of the numerical methods, filter implementation, and subfilter scale modeling. As our understanding and computational capabilities continue to improve, LES will undoubtedly play an increasingly important role in our understanding of turbulent convection in the ocean and atmosphere.

### 8.4 Reynolds-Averaged Navier-Stokes Simulation

#### 8.4a Basics of Reynolds-Averaged Navier-Stokes Simulation

Reynolds-Averaged Navier-Stokes (RANS) simulations are a class of turbulence models that are based on the Navier-Stokes equations, which describe the motion of fluid. The RANS equations are derived from the Navier-Stokes equations through a process called Reynolds decomposition, where the instantaneous velocity is decomposed into its mean and fluctuating components. This decomposition allows for the averaging of the Navier-Stokes equations, leading to the RANS equations.

The RANS equations are given by:

$$
\frac{\partial \overline{u_i}}{\partial t} + \overline{u_j} \frac{\partial \overline{u_i}}{\partial x_j} = -\frac{1}{\rho} \frac{\partial \overline{p}}{\partial x_i} + \nu \frac{\partial^2 \overline{u_i}}{\partial x_j^2} - \frac{\partial \overline{u'_i u'_j}}{\partial x_j}
$$

where $\overline{u_i}$ and $\overline{u_j}$ are the mean velocity components, $\overline{p}$ is the mean pressure, $\rho$ is the fluid density, $\nu$ is the kinematic viscosity, and $\overline{u'_i u'_j}$ is the Reynolds stress tensor, which represents the effect of the turbulence on the mean flow.

The RANS equations are a set of partial differential equations that need to be solved numerically. The solution of these equations provides the mean velocity and pressure fields, which can be used to calculate quantities of interest such as the turbulent kinetic energy and the Reynolds stresses.

One of the main challenges in RANS simulations is the modeling of the Reynolds stresses. These stresses are unknown and need to be modeled in order to close the RANS equations. Various turbulence models have been developed for this purpose, including the k-epsilon and k-omega models, which are based on the transport equations for the turbulent kinetic energy and its dissipation rate.

In the context of oceanic and atmospheric turbulence, RANS simulations can provide valuable insights into the mean flow characteristics and the turbulence structure. However, it should be noted that RANS simulations are based on the assumption of statistical steadiness and homogeneity, which may not be valid in all cases. Therefore, the results of RANS simulations should be interpreted with caution, and the choice of turbulence model should be carefully considered based on the specific characteristics of the flow being studied.

#### 8.4b Reynolds-Averaged Navier-Stokes Simulation of Turbulent Flows

In the simulation of turbulent flows, the Reynolds-Averaged Navier-Stokes (RANS) equations play a crucial role. The RANS equations, as previously discussed, are derived from the Navier-Stokes equations through Reynolds decomposition. This decomposition allows for the averaging of the Navier-Stokes equations, leading to the RANS equations. 

The RANS equations are given by:

$$
\frac{\partial \overline{u_i}}{\partial t} + \overline{u_j} \frac{\partial \overline{u_i}}{\partial x_j} = -\frac{1}{\rho} \frac{\partial \overline{p}}{\partial x_i} + \nu \frac{\partial^2 \overline{u_i}}{\partial x_j^2} - \frac{\partial \overline{u'_i u'_j}}{\partial x_j}
$$

where $\overline{u_i}$ and $\overline{u_j}$ are the mean velocity components, $\overline{p}$ is the mean pressure, $\rho$ is the fluid density, $\nu$ is the kinematic viscosity, and $\overline{u'_i u'_j}$ is the Reynolds stress tensor, which represents the effect of the turbulence on the mean flow.

The RANS equations are a set of partial differential equations that need to be solved numerically. The solution of these equations provides the mean velocity and pressure fields, which can be used to calculate quantities of interest such as the turbulent kinetic energy and the Reynolds stresses.

One of the main challenges in RANS simulations is the modeling of the Reynolds stresses. These stresses are unknown and need to be modeled in order to close the RANS equations. Various turbulence models have been developed for this purpose, including the k-epsilon and k-omega models, which are based on the transport equations for the turbulent kinetic energy and its dissipation rate.

In the context of oceanic and atmospheric turbulence, RANS simulations can provide valuable insights into the dynamics of turbulent flows. These simulations can help us understand the complex interactions between different scales of motion in the ocean and atmosphere, and can provide valuable information for the prediction of weather and climate patterns.

However, it is important to note that RANS simulations have their limitations. They are based on the assumption of statistical homogeneity and isotropy, which may not always be valid in the ocean and atmosphere. Furthermore, the accuracy of RANS simulations depends on the accuracy of the turbulence models used to model the Reynolds stresses. Despite these limitations, RANS simulations remain a powerful tool in the study of turbulence in the ocean and atmosphere.

#### 8.4c Reynolds-Averaged Navier-Stokes Simulation of Turbulent Boundary Layers

The turbulent boundary layer is a region of fluid flow that is of particular interest in the study of turbulence in the ocean and atmosphere. The Reynolds-Averaged Navier-Stokes (RANS) simulations can be used to model these turbulent boundary layers, providing valuable insights into their behavior and dynamics.

The turbulent boundary layer is characterized by a high level of turbulence near the boundary, which gradually decreases as we move away from the boundary. The RANS equations, which we have discussed in the previous section, can be used to model this behavior. However, the modeling of the Reynolds stresses, which represent the effect of the turbulence on the mean flow, is a significant challenge in these simulations.

In the context of the RANS simulations, the turbulent boundary layer can be modeled using wall functions. These functions provide a relationship between the mean velocity and the distance from the wall, allowing for the modeling of the velocity profile in the boundary layer. The use of wall functions significantly reduces the computational cost of the simulations, as it allows for a coarser grid to be used near the wall.

The wall functions are derived from the law of the wall, which is an empirical law that describes the behavior of the mean velocity in the turbulent boundary layer. The law of the wall states that the mean velocity varies logarithmically with the distance from the wall. This law can be expressed as:

$$
\overline{u} = \frac{1}{\kappa} \ln \left( \frac{y}{y_0} \right)
$$

where $\overline{u}$ is the mean velocity, $y$ is the distance from the wall, $y_0$ is the roughness length, and $\kappa$ is the von Karman constant.

The wall functions are implemented in the RANS simulations by modifying the boundary conditions for the mean velocity. This modification allows for the accurate representation of the velocity profile in the turbulent boundary layer, leading to more accurate simulations of turbulent flows.

In the context of oceanic and atmospheric turbulence, the modeling of the turbulent boundary layers is of great importance. These layers play a crucial role in the transport of momentum, heat, and other quantities in the ocean and atmosphere. Therefore, the accurate modeling of these layers can significantly enhance our understanding of the dynamics of turbulent flows in these environments.

#### 8.4d Reynolds-Averaged Navier-Stokes Simulation of Turbulent Convection

Turbulent convection, a phenomenon that occurs in both the ocean and atmosphere, is characterized by the chaotic movement of fluid due to heat transfer. The Reynolds-Averaged Navier-Stokes (RANS) simulations can be used to model this complex process, providing valuable insights into the dynamics of turbulent convection.

In the context of RANS simulations, turbulent convection can be modeled using the Boussinesq approximation. This approximation simplifies the Navier-Stokes equations by assuming that the fluid density is constant, except in the buoyancy term where it is proportional to the temperature difference. This simplification allows for the modeling of the buoyancy-driven flow, which is a key feature of turbulent convection.

The Boussinesq approximation can be expressed as:

$$
\rho = \rho_0 (1 - \beta (T - T_0))
$$

where $\rho$ is the fluid density, $\rho_0$ is the reference density, $\beta$ is the thermal expansion coefficient, $T$ is the fluid temperature, and $T_0$ is the reference temperature.

The RANS equations, combined with the Boussinesq approximation, can be used to model the velocity and temperature fields in turbulent convection. However, similar to the case of turbulent boundary layers, the modeling of the Reynolds stresses and the turbulent heat fluxes, which represent the effect of the turbulence on the mean flow and temperature, is a significant challenge in these simulations.

In the context of the RANS simulations, the Reynolds stresses and the turbulent heat fluxes can be modeled using turbulence models, such as the k-epsilon model or the k-omega model. These models provide closure to the RANS equations by expressing the Reynolds stresses and the turbulent heat fluxes in terms of the mean flow and temperature variables and the turbulence kinetic energy and its dissipation rate.

The k-epsilon model, for example, can be expressed as:

$$
\tau_{ij} = -2 \mu_t S_{ij} + \frac{2}{3} k \delta_{ij}
$$

$$
q_i = - \rho C_p \frac{\mu_t}{\sigma_T} \frac{\partial T}{\partial x_i}
$$

where $\tau_{ij}$ is the Reynolds stress, $\mu_t$ is the turbulent viscosity, $S_{ij}$ is the mean strain rate, $k$ is the turbulence kinetic energy, $\delta_{ij}$ is the Kronecker delta, $q_i$ is the turbulent heat flux, $\rho$ is the fluid density, $C_p$ is the specific heat at constant pressure, $\sigma_T$ is the turbulent Prandtl number, and $T$ is the fluid temperature.

The use of turbulence models in the RANS simulations allows for the accurate representation of the velocity and temperature fields in turbulent convection, providing valuable insights into the dynamics of this complex process.

### Conclusion

Throughout this chapter, we have delved into the complex and fascinating world of turbulence modeling in both the ocean and atmosphere. We have explored the fundamental principles that govern turbulence, the mathematical models used to describe it, and the practical implications of these models in predicting and understanding weather patterns and ocean currents. 

We have seen how turbulence, despite its chaotic nature, follows certain patterns and behaviors that can be mathematically modeled. These models, while not perfect, provide us with valuable tools for predicting and understanding the behavior of the ocean and atmosphere. 

We have also discussed the limitations and challenges of turbulence modeling. Despite the advances in computational power and mathematical techniques, accurately predicting turbulence remains a significant challenge due to its inherently unpredictable nature. However, with continued research and development, we can hope to improve our understanding and prediction of turbulence in the future.

In conclusion, turbulence modeling is a vital field of study with significant implications for our understanding of the world around us. While it presents many challenges, it also offers many opportunities for further research and discovery.

### Exercises

#### Exercise 2

#### Exercise 3
Discuss the practical implications of turbulence modeling. How is it used in predicting and understanding weather patterns and ocean currents?
