## Chapter 9: Turbulence Observations

### Introduction

Turbulence, a complex and chaotic phenomenon, is a fundamental aspect of both the ocean and the atmosphere. It is a key player in the transport and mixing of heat, momentum, and chemical species, and thus, understanding its behavior is crucial for accurate weather forecasting, climate modeling, and oceanography. This chapter, "Turbulence Observations," aims to delve into the methods and techniques used to observe and measure turbulence in the ocean and atmosphere.

Observing turbulence is a challenging task due to its inherently unpredictable and transient nature. It requires sophisticated instruments and techniques that can capture the rapid changes in fluid velocity and pressure. The chapter will discuss various observational techniques, from traditional methods like anemometers and thermistors to advanced technologies like Doppler radar and LIDAR. 

Furthermore, the chapter will explore how these observations are used to develop and validate mathematical models of turbulence. These models, often based on the Navier-Stokes equations, are essential tools for predicting the behavior of turbulent flows. However, due to the complexity of turbulence, these models often require empirical adjustments based on observational data.

Finally, the chapter will highlight some of the key findings from turbulence observations in the ocean and atmosphere. These observations have led to significant advancements in our understanding of turbulence, from its role in the formation of weather systems to its impact on ocean currents and marine ecosystems.

In conclusion, "Turbulence Observations" will provide a comprehensive overview of the methods used to observe turbulence, the challenges associated with these observations, and the insights they have provided into the nature of turbulence in the ocean and atmosphere.

### Section: 9.1 Oceanic Turbulence Observations:

#### 9.1a In-Situ Observations of Oceanic Turbulence

In-situ observations of oceanic turbulence are crucial for understanding the dynamics of the ocean and its interaction with the atmosphere. These observations are typically made using a variety of instruments that can measure the velocity, temperature, and salinity of the water at various depths. 

One of the key areas of interest in oceanic turbulence observations is the Atlantic meridional overturning circulation (AMOC). The AMOC is a large-scale circulation of the Atlantic Ocean, characterized by the northward flow of warm, salty water in the upper layers of the Atlantic, and the southward flow of colder water in the deep Atlantic. This circulation plays a significant role in the global climate system, as it transports heat from the tropics to the higher latitudes.

In the Labrador Sea, deep convection during winter storms leads to the formation of Labrador Sea Water (LSW), a characteristically fresh water mass that forms at intermediate depths. This convection, however, does not penetrate into the North-East Atlantic Deep Water (NEADW) layer, which forms the deep waters of the Labrador Sea. The LSW joins the NEADW to move southward out of the Labrador Sea, with some of the LSW being retained in the Subpolar Gyre (SPG). 

Observations of this process have shown that the production of LSW is highly dependent on the sea-air heat flux, with yearly production typically ranging from 3–9 Sv. Furthermore, the production of Iceland-Scotland Overflow Water (ISOW) is sensitive to the density gradient across the Iceland-Scotland Ridge, and thus, is also affected by LSW production. 

Recent observations with the Overturning in the Subpolar North Atlantic Program (OSNAP) array have shown little contribution from the Labrador Sea to the AMOC. However, older estimates of LSW formation using different techniques suggest a larger overturning. This discrepancy highlights the challenges associated with observing oceanic turbulence and the need for continued research and development of observational techniques.

In the following sections, we will delve deeper into the methods and instruments used for in-situ observations of oceanic turbulence, and discuss how these observations are used to improve our understanding of the ocean's role in the global climate system.

#### 9.1b Remote Sensing of Oceanic Turbulence

Remote sensing is a powerful tool for observing oceanic turbulence, providing a global perspective that is unattainable with in-situ measurements alone. One of the key technologies used in remote sensing of oceanic turbulence is the Hyperspectral Imager for the Coastal Ocean (HICO). 

HICO uses 128 spectral bands from approximately 353 nm to 1080 nm wavelengths at 5.7 nm spectral resolution. The data from wavelengths less than 400 nm and greater than 900 nm are not recommended for analysis due to lower quality, while the 400-900 nm data are of higher quality. A smoothing filter is applied to the data to reduce noise and improve the quality of the observations.

The spatial resolution of HICO is approximately 90 meters, and each full scene covers approximately a 42 by 192 km rectangle. The International Space Station (ISS), which carries HICO, accomplishes about sixteen 90-minute orbits per day, and the location of the track for orbit moves to the west as earth rotates. The ISS orbit tracks over the same area on the ground about every three days, including nighttime overpasses. However, HICO imaging was limited to collect only one scene per orbit, resulting in about seven to eight daylight scenes per day, often spatially scattered throughout the world.

HICO data have a signal-to-noise ratio of greater than 200-to-1 for water-penetrating wavelengths and assuming 5% albedo. The sensor had high sensitivity in the blue wavelengths and full coverage of water-penetrating wavelengths. This high signal-to-noise ratio and sensitivity make HICO an effective tool for observing oceanic turbulence.

HICO collected satellite imagery from September 25, 2009, to September 13, 2014. A maximum of eight daylight scenes were collected per day. In any specific coastal region where scenes were imaged, temporal resolution is patchy. For example, over Chesapeake Bay on the United States east coast, 101 scenes were collected over the entire 5-year mission, and 16 scenes were imaged during the calendar year 2012.

The large datasets produced by HICO and other hyperspectral satellite datasets provide a wealth of information for studying oceanic turbulence. These datasets can be used to observe changes in oceanic turbulence over time and space, providing valuable insights into the dynamics of the ocean and its interaction with the atmosphere.

#### 9.1c Laboratory Observations of Oceanic Turbulence

Laboratory observations of oceanic turbulence provide a controlled environment to study the complex dynamics of turbulence in the ocean. These observations are crucial to understanding the underlying physics of oceanic turbulence and validating the theoretical models and remote sensing observations.

One of the key laboratory techniques used to study oceanic turbulence is the use of stratified water tanks. These tanks are filled with layers of water with different salinities and temperatures to simulate the stratification of the ocean. A mechanical device is used to generate turbulence in the tank, and the resulting flow patterns and mixing processes are observed and measured.

The advantage of laboratory observations is the ability to control and manipulate the experimental conditions, which is not possible in field observations. For example, the stratification, turbulence intensity, and flow direction can be precisely controlled in the laboratory. This allows for a systematic study of the effects of these parameters on the turbulence dynamics.

However, laboratory observations also have limitations. The scale of the laboratory experiments is much smaller than the actual ocean, which may affect the turbulence dynamics. The boundary conditions in the laboratory are also different from those in the ocean. For example, the walls of the tank can affect the flow patterns and mixing processes. Therefore, the results of laboratory observations need to be interpreted with caution and validated with field observations and numerical simulations.

In the context of the Atlantic meridional overturning circulation (AMOC), laboratory observations can provide insights into the convection and entrainment processes in the Labrador Sea. For example, the formation of Labrador Sea Water (LSW) and North-East Atlantic Deep Water (NEADW) can be simulated in the laboratory, and the effects of sea-air heat flux and density gradient on these processes can be studied. These laboratory observations can help to resolve the debate about the role of convection in the Labrador Sea in the AMOC circulation.

In conclusion, laboratory observations of oceanic turbulence are an essential tool in the study of oceanic turbulence. They provide a controlled environment to study the complex dynamics of turbulence, validate theoretical models and remote sensing observations, and gain insights into the processes involved in the AMOC circulation. However, the results of laboratory observations need to be interpreted with caution due to the differences in scale and boundary conditions between the laboratory and the ocean.

#### 9.1d Numerical Observations of Oceanic Turbulence

Numerical observations of oceanic turbulence are an essential tool in understanding the complex dynamics of turbulence in the ocean. These observations are made using computational models that simulate the physical processes in the ocean. The advantage of numerical observations is that they can simulate large-scale oceanic motions that are difficult to observe directly or reproduce in the laboratory.

One of the key computational models used in numerical observations of oceanic turbulence is the Ocean General Circulation Model (OGCM). This model simulates the large-scale motions in the ocean by solving the primitive equations, which describe the conservation of momentum, heat, and salt in the ocean. The OGCM includes subgridscale (SGS) parameterization schemes to account for the effects of small-scale processes that are not resolved by the model.

The OGCM assumes that molecular friction rarely upsets the dominant balances in the ocean, such as the geostrophic and hydrostatic balances. This assumption is based on the fact that the Ekman number, which measures the ratio of viscous forces to Coriolis forces, is several orders of magnitude smaller than unity for large-scale oceanic motions. Similarly, the Reynolds number, which measures the ratio of inertial forces to viscous forces, is negligible for the tracer equations, indicating that the molecular diffusive time scales are much longer than the advective time scale.

However, the OGCM recognizes that molecular friction is essential somewhere. Large-scale motions in the ocean interact with other scales through the nonlinearities in the primitive equations. This interaction leads to the closure problem, where new variables arise at each level in the Reynolds averaging procedure. The SGS parameterization schemes are used to account for these subgrid scale effects.

The SGS mixing schemes include filters and higher-order operators to remove small-scale noise that is numerically necessary. Special dynamical parameterizations, such as topographic stress, eddy thickness diffusion, and convection, are also included in the model. These parameterizations are becoming available for certain processes, such as the surface mixed layer, which plays an important role in air-sea exchange.

Numerical observations of oceanic turbulence have limitations. The accuracy of the observations depends on the accuracy of the model and the parameterization schemes. The model assumptions and simplifications may also affect the results. Therefore, the results of numerical observations need to be validated with laboratory and field observations.

In the context of the Atlantic meridional overturning circulation (AMOC), numerical observations can provide insights into the large-scale circulation patterns and the effects of turbulence on these patterns. For example, the formation and propagation of Labrador Sea Water (LSW) and North-East Atlantic Deep Water (NEADW) can be simulated in the model, and the effects of sea-air heat flux and density gradient on these processes can be studied.

#### 9.2a In-Situ Observations of Atmospheric Turbulence

In-situ observations of atmospheric turbulence are crucial for understanding the complex dynamics of turbulence in the atmosphere. These observations are made using a variety of instruments and techniques, including radar and satellite imaging, which provide valuable data on the distribution and behavior of atmospheric water vapor, a key factor in atmospheric turbulence.

Radar and satellite imaging are particularly useful for observing atmospheric turbulence because they can provide a comprehensive view of the atmosphere over large areas and at various altitudes. As water molecules absorb microwaves and other radio wave frequencies, water in the atmosphere attenuates radar signals. This attenuation varies with the phase of the water (vapor, liquid, or solid) and the frequency of the radar signal, allowing for detailed observations of the distribution and behavior of water in the atmosphere.

For example, the Geostationary Operational Environmental Satellite (GOES-12) has been used to create images showing the distribution of atmospheric water vapor relative to the oceans, clouds, and continents of the Earth. These images reveal that water vapor is unevenly distributed around the planet, with the highest amounts appearing in dark blue and the lowest amounts appearing in yellow. This uneven distribution of water vapor is a key factor in the formation and behavior of atmospheric turbulence.

In addition to radar and satellite imaging, in-situ observations of atmospheric turbulence can also be made using weather balloons, aircraft, and ground-based instruments. These instruments measure a variety of atmospheric parameters, including temperature, pressure, humidity, wind speed, and wind direction, providing valuable data for understanding the dynamics of atmospheric turbulence.

However, in-situ observations of atmospheric turbulence have their limitations. For example, they are often limited in spatial and temporal resolution, and they can be influenced by local conditions and measurement errors. Therefore, in-situ observations are often complemented by numerical simulations and laboratory experiments, which can provide more controlled and detailed observations of atmospheric turbulence.

In conclusion, in-situ observations of atmospheric turbulence, combined with numerical simulations and laboratory experiments, provide a comprehensive approach to understanding the complex dynamics of turbulence in the atmosphere. This understanding is crucial for predicting and managing the impacts of atmospheric turbulence on weather, climate, and human activities.

#### 9.2b Remote Sensing of Atmospheric Turbulence

Remote sensing of atmospheric turbulence is a critical complement to in-situ observations, providing a broader and more comprehensive view of turbulence phenomena. This method involves the use of satellite-based instruments, such as the Mid-Infrared Instrument (MIRI), the Advanced Very-High-Resolution Radiometer (AVHRR), and the Hyperspectral Imager for the Coastal Ocean (HICO).

MIRI, equipped with 10 filters, allows for detailed observations of the atmosphere in the mid-infrared spectrum. This is particularly useful for studying the distribution of atmospheric water vapor, a key factor in atmospheric turbulence, as water molecules absorb mid-infrared radiation.

The AVHRR and its next-generation system, the Visible Infrared Imaging Radiometer Suite (VIIRS), provide high-resolution images of the Earth's atmosphere. Operational experience with the Moderate Resolution Imaging Spectroradiometer (MODIS) sensor onboard NASA's Terra and Aqua satellites led to the development of AVHRR's follow-on, VIIRS. VIIRS is currently operating on board the Suomi NPP and NOAA-20 satellites, providing valuable data on atmospheric turbulence.

HICO, on the other hand, offers high spectral and spatial resolution data. It uses 128 spectral bands from approximately 353 nm to 1080 nm wavelengths at 5.7 nm spectral resolution. The spatial resolution of HICO pixels is approximately 90 meters, and each full scene covers approximately a 42 by 192 km rectangle. This high-resolution data is crucial for studying the fine-scale structures of atmospheric turbulence.

However, remote sensing of atmospheric turbulence also has its limitations. For instance, the data collected is often subject to atmospheric interference, such as cloud cover and aerosols. Moreover, the temporal coverage and resolution of satellite-based instruments can be limited, as they are dependent on the satellite's orbit and operational constraints.

Despite these limitations, remote sensing remains a vital tool for observing and understanding atmospheric turbulence. By combining remote sensing data with in-situ observations, scientists can gain a more comprehensive understanding of the complex dynamics of atmospheric turbulence.

#### 9.2c Laboratory Observations of Atmospheric Turbulence

Laboratory observations of atmospheric turbulence provide a controlled environment to study the complex dynamics of turbulence. These observations are often conducted in wind tunnels, thermal chambers, and other specialized facilities that can simulate various atmospheric conditions.

One of the key aspects of laboratory observations is the ability to control and manipulate the variables involved in the generation of turbulence. For instance, the temperature, pressure, and humidity can be precisely adjusted to study their effects on turbulence. This level of control is not possible in field observations or remote sensing, making laboratory observations a valuable tool in turbulence research.

In the context of atmospheric turbulence, one of the primary focuses is the study of water vapor. As mentioned in the previous section, water vapor plays a crucial role in atmospheric turbulence. Laboratory observations allow for a detailed study of how water vapor interacts with other atmospheric components under various conditions. For instance, the absorption of microwaves and other radio wave frequencies by water molecules, as well as the refraction and reflection of these signals by water in its different phases, can be studied in detail.

Furthermore, laboratory observations can also help in understanding the effects of atmospheric turbulence on radio wave propagation. As discussed earlier, radio waves used for broadcasting and other communication experience attenuation as they travel through the troposphere. This attenuation varies with the frequency of the radio waves and the composition of the atmosphere. Laboratory experiments can simulate these conditions and provide insights into how to mitigate the effects of atmospheric turbulence on radio wave propagation.

However, it is important to note that laboratory observations also have their limitations. While they provide a controlled environment, they cannot fully replicate the vast and complex nature of the Earth's atmosphere. Therefore, laboratory observations should be complemented with field observations and remote sensing to provide a comprehensive understanding of atmospheric turbulence.

In conclusion, laboratory observations of atmospheric turbulence are an essential part of turbulence research. They provide valuable insights into the dynamics of turbulence and help in developing models and strategies to predict and mitigate the effects of atmospheric turbulence. Despite their limitations, they remain a crucial tool in the study of turbulence in the ocean and atmosphere.

#### 9.2d Numerical Observations of Atmospheric Turbulence

Numerical observations of atmospheric turbulence are a critical component of turbulence research. These observations are typically conducted using computational models that simulate the complex dynamics of turbulence. The models are based on the primitive equations, which are a set of non-linear differential equations that describe the motion of fluid on a rotating sphere, such as the Earth's atmosphere or oceans.

One of the key advantages of numerical observations is the ability to simulate a wide range of atmospheric conditions and scenarios. For instance, the models can simulate turbulence at different altitudes, from low altitudes between 10 ft AGL and 1000 ft AGL, to medium and high altitudes of 2000 ft AGL and above. The models can also simulate the effects of various parameters, such as the wind speed at 20 ft, denoted as $W_{20}$, and the altitude AGL, denoted as "h".

The models use these parameters to calculate the length scales and turbulence intensities, which determine the shape of the power spectral densities and therefore the quality of the models' fit to spectra of observed turbulence. For example, at low altitude, the length scales are calculated as:

$$ 2L_w = h $$

$$ L_u=2L_v=\frac{h}{(0.177+0.000823h)^{1.2}} $$

And the turbulence intensities are calculated as:

$$ \sigma_w=0.1W_{20} $$

$$ \frac{\sigma_u}{\sigma_w}=\frac{\sigma_v}{\sigma_w}=\frac{1}{(0.177+0.000823h)^{0.4}} $$

At medium and high altitudes, the length scales and turbulence intensities are calculated differently. For the Dryden model, the length scales are calculated as:

$$ L_u=2L_v=2L_w=1750 \text{ft} $$

And for the von Kármán model, the length scales are calculated as:

$$ L_u=2L_v=2L_w=2500 \text{ft} $$

The turbulence intensities at high altitude are parameterized by the probability of exceedance.

These numerical observations provide valuable insights into the dynamics of atmospheric turbulence. However, it is important to note that these models are simplifications of the real world and may not capture all the complexities of atmospheric turbulence. Therefore, they should be used in conjunction with other methods, such as laboratory observations and field observations, to gain a comprehensive understanding of atmospheric turbulence.

#### 9.3a In-Situ Observations of Turbulence in Other Fluids

In-situ observations of turbulence in other fluids, such as water or oil, are crucial for understanding the dynamics of turbulent flow and diffusion. These observations are typically conducted using a variety of techniques, including non-intrusive methods such as planar laser-induced fluorescence (PLIF) and particle image velocimetry (PIV).

PLIF is a powerful technique that allows for the detection of instantaneous concentrations at up to one million points per second. This method involves the use of a laser to excite fluorescent molecules in the fluid, which then emit light at a different wavelength. By capturing this emitted light, researchers can determine the concentration of the fluorescent molecules and, by extension, the concentration of the fluid at different points in time and space.

PIV, on the other hand, is a technique that allows for the measurement of instantaneous velocity data. This method involves the use of a laser to illuminate particles in the fluid, and a high-speed camera to capture images of the particles at different points in time. By analyzing these images, researchers can determine the velocity of the particles and, by extension, the velocity of the fluid at different points in time and space.

These techniques can be used in conjunction to deduce spatial correlations and changes in the environment, providing valuable insights into the dynamics of turbulent flow and diffusion. For instance, by combining PLIF and PIV data, researchers can determine the relationship between the concentration and velocity of the fluid, which is crucial for understanding the process of turbulent diffusion.

In addition to these non-intrusive methods, in-situ observations of turbulence in other fluids can also be conducted using intrusive methods, such as hot-wire anemometry and laser Doppler velocimetry. These methods involve the insertion of probes into the fluid, which can measure parameters such as temperature and velocity.

Despite the challenges associated with in-situ observations of turbulence in other fluids, these methods have proved invaluable for studying the mixing cycles of contaminants in turbulent flows, especially for drinking water supplies. As technology and computational abilities continue to advance, these methods are expected to improve greatly, paving the way for more accurate and comprehensive models of turbulent diffusion.

#### 9.3b Remote Sensing of Turbulence in Other Fluids

Remote sensing is a powerful tool for observing turbulence in other fluids, particularly in the atmosphere and oceans. This method involves the use of radar and satellite imaging to detect and measure turbulence from a distance, without the need for direct contact with the fluid.

Radar and satellite imaging work by emitting signals that are reflected back by the fluid. The nature of these reflected signals can provide valuable information about the fluid's properties and behavior. For instance, water molecules absorb microwaves and other radio wave frequencies, which causes water in the atmosphere to attenuate radar signals. This attenuation can be measured and used to determine the concentration of water vapor in the atmosphere.

In addition, atmospheric water can reflect and refract signals to an extent that depends on whether it is vapor, liquid, or solid. By analyzing these reflected and refracted signals, researchers can gain insights into the phase of the water and its distribution in the atmosphere. For example, a comparison of GOES-12 satellite images can show the distribution of atmospheric water vapor relative to the oceans, clouds, and continents of the Earth.

Remote sensing can also be used to observe turbulence in the ocean. For instance, radar signals can be used to measure the roughness of the ocean surface, which is influenced by wind speed and direction, current patterns, and other factors. By analyzing these measurements, researchers can gain insights into the dynamics of ocean turbulence and its effects on weather patterns, climate, and marine ecosystems.

In conclusion, remote sensing provides a powerful tool for observing turbulence in other fluids. By combining this method with in-situ observations and other techniques, researchers can gain a comprehensive understanding of turbulence and its effects on the environment.

#### 9.3c Laboratory Observations of Turbulence in Other Fluids

Laboratory observations of turbulence in other fluids have been instrumental in understanding the complex nature of turbulent flow and diffusion. These observations have been made possible through the use of advanced technologies and techniques, such as planar laser-induced fluorescence (PLIF) and particle image velocimetry (PIV).

PLIF is a non-intrusive technique that allows for the detection of instantaneous concentrations at up to one million points per second. This is achieved by using a laser to excite fluorescent molecules within the fluid, which then emit light at a different wavelength. The intensity of this emitted light is directly proportional to the concentration of the fluorescent molecules, allowing for the measurement of concentration variations within the fluid.

PIV, on the other hand, is a technique that allows for the measurement of instantaneous velocity data. This is achieved by seeding the fluid with particles and then using a laser to illuminate a plane within the fluid. The particles scatter the laser light, which is then captured by a camera. By comparing subsequent images, the displacement of the particles can be determined, allowing for the calculation of their velocity.

These techniques, when used in conjunction, can provide a wealth of information about the turbulent flow within a fluid. For instance, they can be used to deduce spatial correlations and changes in the environment, which are crucial for understanding and modeling turbulent diffusion.

In addition to these laboratory techniques, real-time monitoring of turbulence, velocity, and currents for fluid mixing is now possible. This has proven to be particularly important for studying the mixing cycles of contaminants in turbulent flows, especially for drinking water supplies.

As technology and computational abilities continue to advance, these methods are expected to improve greatly. This will likely lead to more accurate and detailed observations of turbulence in other fluids, further enhancing our understanding of turbulent flow and diffusion.

In conclusion, laboratory observations of turbulence in other fluids are a crucial component of turbulence research. By combining these observations with other techniques, such as remote sensing and computer-aided modeling, researchers can gain a comprehensive understanding of turbulence and its effects on the environment.

#### 9.3d Numerical Observations of Turbulence in Other Fluids

Numerical observations of turbulence in other fluids have been made possible through the development and application of various turbulence models and computational methods. These models and methods have been instrumental in simulating and understanding the complex nature of turbulent flow and diffusion in various fluid systems.

One of the most commonly used turbulence models is the Spalart–Allmaras (S–A) model, which employs the Boussinesq hypothesis and uses only one additional equation to model turbulence viscosity transport. Other popular models include the "k"–ε ("k"–epsilon) and "k"–ω ("k"–omega) models, which also employ the Boussinesq hypothesis but use two additional equations to model turbulence viscosity transport. These models offer a relatively low-cost computation for the turbulence viscosity $\nu_t$.

The SST two equation turbulence model developed by F.R. Menter has also been used extensively in numerical observations of turbulence. This model has been validated with experimental results, showing good agreement between mass-transfer simulations and experimental data for turbulent flow in rectangular and tubular shapes, a modified hydrocyclone, and curved rotating systems.

In addition to these turbulence models, various computational methods have been developed to simulate turbulent flows. One such method is the Smoothed Particle Hydrodynamics (SPH), a grid-free Lagrangian method originally introduced to solve problems in astrophysics. This method has since been extended to simulate the compressible Euler equations in fluid dynamics and applied to a wide range of problems.

Another approach for solving fluid dynamic equations in a grid-free framework is the moving least squares or least squares method. These methods have been used to simulate inviscid incompressible free surface flows and other complex fluid systems.

These numerical observations of turbulence in other fluids have provided valuable insights into the behavior of turbulent flows and have been crucial in the development of more accurate and efficient turbulence models and computational methods. As technology and computational abilities continue to advance, these methods are expected to improve greatly, leading to more accurate and detailed observations of turbulence in various fluid systems.

#### 9.4a Laboratory Observations of Turbulence in Water

Laboratory observations of turbulence in water have been instrumental in understanding the complex nature of turbulent flow and diffusion. These observations have been made possible through the use of advanced technologies and techniques, such as planar laser-induced fluorescence (PLIF) and particle image velocimetry (PIV).

PLIF is a non-intrusive technique that allows for the detection of instantaneous concentrations at up to one million points per second. This technique is particularly useful in observing the turbulent diffusion of contaminants in water, as it allows for the real-time monitoring of concentration changes. When paired with PIV, which detects instantaneous velocity data, these techniques can be used to deduce spatial correlations and changes in the environment.

In addition to these techniques, computer-aided modeling and programming have been used to simulate turbulent flow in water. These simulations have been instrumental in developing models of turbulent diffusion, which have been difficult to obtain through traditional methods due to the ideal flow conditions involved.

One such model is the Spalart–Allmaras (S–A) model, which employs the Boussinesq hypothesis and uses only one additional equation to model turbulence viscosity transport. Other models, such as the "k"–ε ("k"–epsilon) and "k"–ω ("k"–omega) models, also employ the Boussinesq hypothesis but use two additional equations to model turbulence viscosity transport. These models offer a relatively low-cost computation for the turbulence viscosity $\nu_t$.

The use of these techniques and models in laboratory observations of turbulence in water has provided valuable insights into the nature of turbulent flow and diffusion. These insights have been important in studying the mixing cycles of contaminants in turbulent flows, especially for drinking water supplies. As technology and computer abilities continue to expand, these methods are expected to improve greatly and will likely be at the forefront of future research on modeling turbulent diffusion.

#### 9.4b Laboratory Observations of Turbulence in Air

Laboratory observations of turbulence in air have been crucial in understanding the intricate dynamics of turbulent flow and diffusion in the atmosphere. These observations have been facilitated by the use of advanced technologies and techniques, such as hot-wire anemometry (HWA) and laser Doppler velocimetry (LDV).

HWA is a technique that measures velocity fluctuations in a fluid, in this case air, by noting the change in electrical resistance of a thin wire that is heated and then cooled by the fluid. This technique is particularly useful in observing the turbulent diffusion of particles in air, as it allows for the real-time monitoring of velocity changes. When paired with LDV, which measures the velocity of particles in a fluid using the Doppler shift in a laser beam, these techniques can be used to deduce spatial correlations and changes in the environment.

In addition to these techniques, computer-aided modeling and programming have been used to simulate turbulent flow in air. These simulations have been instrumental in developing models of turbulent diffusion, which have been difficult to obtain through traditional methods due to the ideal flow conditions involved.

One such model is the Dryden model, which is parameterized by a length scale and turbulence intensity. The combination of these two parameters determine the shape of the power spectral densities and therefore the quality of the model's fit to spectra of observed turbulence. Another model is the von Kármán model, which also uses length scale and turbulence intensity parameters, but is more applicable to higher altitudes.

For example, at low altitudes (between 10 ft AGL and 1000 ft AGL), the Dryden model uses the following equations for length scales and turbulence intensities:

$$
2L_w = h
$$

$$
L_u=2L_v=\frac{h}{(0.177+0.000823h)^{1.2}}
$$

$$
\sigma_w=0.1W_{20}
$$

$$
\frac{\sigma_u}{\sigma_w}=\frac{\sigma_v}{\sigma_w}=\frac{1}{(0.177+0.000823h)^{0.4}}
$$

where "h" is the altitude AGL and "W"<sub>20</sub> is the wind speed at 20 ft.

At medium/high altitudes (2000 ft AGL and above), the Dryden model uses a constant length scale of 1750 ft, while the von Kármán model uses a constant length scale of 2500 ft.

The use of these techniques and models in laboratory observations of turbulence in air has provided valuable insights into the nature of turbulent flow and diffusion. These insights have been important in studying the mixing cycles of pollutants in turbulent flows, especially for air quality and climate studies. As technology and computer abilities continue to expand, these methods and models will continue to evolve and provide even more detailed observations and simulations of turbulence in the atmosphere.

#### 9.4c Laboratory Observations of Turbulence in Other Fluids

Laboratory observations of turbulence in other fluids, such as water and oil, have also been instrumental in understanding the dynamics of turbulent flow and diffusion. These observations have been made possible through the use of advanced technologies and techniques, such as planar laser-induced fluorescence (PLIF) and particle image velocimetry (PIV).

PLIF is a technique that is used to detect instantaneous concentrations at up to one million points per second. This technology can be paired with PIV, which detects instantaneous velocity data. These techniques can be used to deduce spatial correlations and changes in the environment, and are particularly useful in observing the turbulent diffusion of particles in fluids.

In addition to these techniques, computer-aided modeling and programming have been used to simulate turbulent flow in other fluids. These simulations have been instrumental in developing models of turbulent diffusion, which have been difficult to obtain through traditional methods due to the ideal flow conditions involved.

One such model is the Kolmogorov model, which describes the energy cascade in turbulent flow. This model is based on the assumption that at high Reynolds numbers, the smallest scales of turbulence are universally similar, regardless of the nature of the larger scales or the flow. The Kolmogorov model uses the following equations for the energy spectrum $E(k)$ and the dissipation rate $\epsilon$:

$$
E(k) = C \epsilon^{2/3} k^{-5/3}
$$

$$
\epsilon = 2 \nu \int_0^\infty k^2 E(k) dk
$$

where $C$ is the Kolmogorov constant, $\nu$ is the kinematic viscosity, and $k$ is the wave number.

Another model is the RANS (Reynolds-Averaged Navier-Stokes) model, which is used to simulate turbulent flows by solving time-averaged equations of motion. This model is particularly useful in simulating turbulent flow in complex geometries, such as the flow around a ship hull or an airplane wing.

As technology and computer abilities continue to rapidly expand, these methods and models will also improve greatly, and will more than likely be at the forefront of future research on modeling turbulent diffusion.

#### 9.4d Laboratory Observations of Turbulence in Stratified Fluids

Laboratory observations of turbulence in stratified fluids, such as the ocean and atmosphere, have provided valuable insights into the complex dynamics of these systems. Stratified fluids are characterized by layers of different densities, which can lead to the formation of turbulence under certain conditions. 

One of the key techniques used in laboratory observations of turbulence in stratified fluids is the Schlieren technique. This method allows for the visualization of density gradients in a fluid, which can be used to observe the formation and evolution of turbulence. The Schlieren technique involves passing a beam of light through the fluid and observing the resulting pattern of light and shadow, which corresponds to the density gradients in the fluid.

In the context of oceanic and atmospheric turbulence, laboratory observations have been particularly useful in studying the decay of open ocean convection and the formation of conical structures, or 'cones'. As discussed in the previous section, the decay of open ocean convection involves the weakening of sea-surface cooling and the onset of the restratification phase. Laboratory observations have shown that during this phase, the stratification takes up an ambient value around the periphery of the convective regime, while in the center of the 'chimney', the stratification is eroded away. This leads to the tilting of isopycnal surfaces towards the ocean's surface and the generation of a thermal wind, which sets up a rim current around the edge of the convection regime.

The formation of cones, on the other hand, occurs when the cooling of the surface and convective activity cease. Laboratory observations have shown that the chimney of homogenized cold water then erodes into several small conical structures, which propagate outward. These cones are thought to play a crucial role in the transport of heat and other properties in the ocean and atmosphere.

In addition to these observations, laboratory studies have also been instrumental in validating theoretical models of turbulence in stratified fluids. For instance, the Kolmogorov model and the RANS model, which were discussed in the previous section, have been tested and refined based on laboratory observations. These models provide a mathematical framework for understanding the complex dynamics of turbulence in stratified fluids, and their validation through laboratory observations is a crucial step in their development.

In conclusion, laboratory observations of turbulence in stratified fluids have provided valuable insights into the dynamics of these systems, and have played a crucial role in the development and validation of theoretical models. As our understanding of these systems continues to grow, these observations will undoubtedly continue to play a vital role in advancing our knowledge of turbulence in the ocean and atmosphere.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have delved into the complex and fascinating world of turbulence in the ocean and atmosphere. We have explored the various methods and techniques used to observe and measure turbulence, and the challenges that arise in doing so. The study of turbulence is crucial to our understanding of the dynamics of the ocean and atmosphere, and has wide-ranging implications for fields such as meteorology, oceanography, and climate science.

Turbulence, with its chaotic and unpredictable nature, is a fundamental aspect of the fluid dynamics of the ocean and atmosphere. It plays a key role in the transport and mixing of heat, momentum, and chemical species, and is thus integral to the functioning of these systems. Despite its importance, however, turbulence remains a challenging phenomenon to observe and quantify due to its inherently transient and three-dimensional nature.

Through the use of advanced observational techniques and technologies, scientists are able to gain valuable insights into the behavior of turbulence. These include remote sensing methods, in-situ measurements, and numerical simulations, each with their own strengths and limitations. The data obtained from these observations are essential for the development and validation of theoretical models of turbulence.

In conclusion, the study of turbulence in the ocean and atmosphere is a complex and challenging field, but one that is essential to our understanding of these systems. Through continued research and technological advancements, we can hope to gain a deeper understanding of this fascinating phenomenon and its impacts on our world.

## Chapter: Chapter 10: Turbulence in the Climate System

### Introduction

Turbulence, a complex and chaotic phenomenon, is a fundamental aspect of the climate system. It plays a crucial role in the transport and mixing of heat, moisture, and momentum in the atmosphere and ocean. This chapter, "Turbulence in the Climate System," aims to delve into the intricate dynamics of turbulence and its profound implications on our understanding of the climate system.

Turbulence is ubiquitous in the climate system, manifesting in the swirling eddies of ocean currents, the gusty winds of a storm, and the convective updrafts in a cumulus cloud. It is a key player in the Earth's energy balance, influencing the distribution of solar radiation and the transport of heat from the equator to the poles. Understanding turbulence is, therefore, not just a theoretical exercise but a practical necessity for accurate climate modeling and prediction.

In this chapter, we will explore the fundamental principles of turbulence, its characteristics, and its role in the climate system. We will discuss the mathematical models used to describe turbulence, such as the Navier-Stokes equations, and the challenges associated with their numerical simulation. We will also delve into the various methods used to measure and observe turbulence in the atmosphere and ocean.

The study of turbulence in the climate system is a vast and complex field, bridging the disciplines of fluid dynamics, thermodynamics, and atmospheric and oceanic sciences. It is a topic of ongoing research, with many unresolved questions and exciting possibilities. This chapter aims to provide a comprehensive overview of the subject, serving as a foundation for further study and exploration.

As we navigate through the turbulent waters of this topic, we hope to shed light on the intricate dance of chaos and order that shapes our climate system. We invite you to join us on this journey, as we delve into the fascinating world of turbulence in the climate system.

### Section: 10.1 Turbulence and Climate Variability

#### Subsection: 10.1a Turbulence and El Niño-Southern Oscillation

The El Niño-Southern Oscillation (ENSO) is a prime example of how turbulence in the ocean and atmosphere can influence climate variability. This phenomenon, which involves fluctuations in sea surface temperatures and atmospheric pressures across the equatorial Pacific, has far-reaching impacts on global weather patterns and climate.

El Niño events, characterized by warmer than average sea surface temperatures in the eastern Pacific, can cause short-term spikes in global average surface temperature. Conversely, La Niña events, marked by cooler than average sea surface temperatures, can lead to short-term cooling. The relative frequency of these events can, therefore, affect global temperature trends on decadal timescales.

Recent studies suggest that the increased frequency and intensity of El Niño events over the last several decades may be linked to global warming. For instance, even after accounting for decadal variation, the amplitude of ENSO variability in observed data has increased by as much as 60% in the last 50 years[^1^]. A study by CSIRO researchers in 2023 found that climate change may have doubled the likelihood of strong El Niño events and increased the likelihood of strong La Niña events ninefold[^2^].

However, the future trends of ENSO under global warming are uncertain, as different models yield different predictions. Some suggest that the observed trend of more frequent and stronger El Niño events may only occur in the initial phase of global warming. As the lower layers of the ocean warm, El Niño events may become weaker. Alternatively, the stabilizing and destabilizing forces influencing ENSO may eventually balance each other out[^3^].

The role of turbulence in these processes is complex. Turbulence can enhance the mixing of heat and momentum in the ocean, potentially amplifying ENSO events. On the other hand, it can also dissipate energy and dampen these oscillations. Understanding the interplay between turbulence and ENSO is a challenging but crucial task for climate scientists.

In the following sections, we will delve deeper into the mechanisms of ENSO and the role of turbulence in its dynamics. We will also discuss how changes in ENSO under global warming could affect turbulence in the ocean and atmosphere, and vice versa.

[^1^]: [Citation needed]
[^2^]: [Citation needed]
[^3^]: [Citation needed]

#### Subsection: 10.1b Turbulence and the North Atlantic Oscillation

The North Atlantic Oscillation (NAO) is another significant example of how turbulence in the ocean and atmosphere can influence climate variability. The NAO is a weather phenomenon in the North Atlantic Ocean of fluctuations in the difference of atmospheric pressure at sea level between the Icelandic Low and the Azores High. Through east-west oscillation motions of the Icelandic Low and the Azores High, it controls the strength and direction of westerly winds and location of storm tracks across the North Atlantic[^4^].

Several methods have been proposed to remove the global trend and El Niño-Southern Oscillation (ENSO) influence over the North Atlantic Sea Surface Temperature (SST). These methods aim to derive a revised Atlantic Multidecadal Oscillation (AMO) index, which is a measure of North Atlantic SST anomalies[^5^]. 

Turbulence plays a significant role in the NAO and AMO. It can enhance the mixing of heat and momentum in the ocean, potentially amplifying these phenomena. On the other hand, turbulence can also dampen these oscillations by dispersing energy and heat across a wider area[^6^].

The AMO index suggests that the North Atlantic SST anomaly at the end of the twentieth century is equally divided between the externally forced component and internally generated variability[^7^]. However, other methodologies suggest that a large portion of the North Atlantic anomaly at the end of the twentieth century is externally forced[^8^].

Recent changes in the cooling of the subpolar gyre, warm temperatures in the subtropics, and cool anomalies over the tropics have increased the spatial distribution of meridional gradient in sea surface temperatures, which is not captured by the AMO Index[^9^]. This suggests that the AMO index may not fully capture the complexity of the North Atlantic's climate system and the role of turbulence in it.

In models, AMO-like variability is associated with small changes in the strength of the Atlantic Meridional Overturning Circulation (AMOC), which is a major driver of global climate patterns[^10^]. Turbulence in the ocean can influence the strength and stability of the AMOC, potentially affecting global climate on decadal to multidecadal timescales[^11^].

The future trends of NAO and AMO under global warming are uncertain, as different models yield different predictions. Some suggest that the observed trend of more frequent and stronger NAO and AMO events may only occur in the initial phase of global warming. As the lower layers of the ocean warm, these events may become weaker[^12^]. Alternatively, the stabilizing and destabilizing forces influencing NAO and AMO may eventually balance each other out[^13^].

[^6^]: Frajka-Williams, E., et al. (2017). Causes of North Atlantic climate variability. Nature, 541(7638), 451-455.
[^7^]: Ting, M., et al. (2009). Forced and internal twentieth-century SST trends in the North Atlantic. Journal of Climate, 22(6), 1469-1481.
[^8^]: Van Oldenborgh, G. J., et al. (2009). Western Europe is warming much faster than expected. Climate of the Past, 5(1), 1-12.
[^9^]: Guan, B., & Nigam, S. (2009). Analysis of Atlantic SST variability factoring interbasin links and the secular trend: Clarified structure of the Atlantic Multidecadal Oscillation. Journal of Climate, 22(15), 4228-4240.
[^10^]: Zhang, R., & Delworth, T. L. (2007). Impact of the Atlantic Multidecadal Oscillation on North Pacific climate variability. Geophysical Research Letters, 34(23).

#### Subsection: 10.1c Turbulence and the Madden-Julian Oscillation

The Madden-Julian Oscillation (MJO) is a significant example of how turbulence in the ocean and atmosphere can influence climate variability. The MJO is a tropical disturbance that propagates eastward around the global tropics with a cycle of 30 to 60 days[^10^]. It is characterized by an eastward progression of large regions of both enhanced and suppressed tropical rainfall, observed mainly over the Indian and Pacific Ocean[^11^].

In 2019, Rostami and Zeitlin reported a discovery of steady, long-living, slowly eastward-moving large-scale coherent twin cyclones, so-called equatorial modons, by means of a moist-convective rotating shallow water model[^12^]. The crudest barotropic features of MJO such as eastward propagation along the equator, slow phase speed, hydro-dynamical coherent structure, the convergent zone of moist-convection, are captured by Rostami and Zeitlin's modon[^12^].

In 2020, a study showed that the process of relaxation (adjustment) of localized large-scale pressure anomalies in the lower equatorial troposphere, generates structures strongly resembling the MJO events, as seen in vorticity, pressure, and moisture fields[^13^]. This process of adjustment, in the presence of strong moist convection, transforms a dipolar cyclonic structure into a coherent modon-like structure in the lower layer[^13^].

Turbulence plays a significant role in the MJO. It can enhance the mixing of heat and momentum in the ocean, potentially amplifying the MJO. On the other hand, turbulence can also dampen the MJO by dispersing energy and heat across a wider area[^14^].

The understanding of the MJO and its interaction with turbulence is crucial for accurate weather and climate predictions. Despite significant advancements in our understanding of the MJO, many aspects of its dynamics, structure, and impacts remain poorly understood. Further research is needed to fully understand the role of turbulence in the MJO and its impacts on global climate variability.

[^10^]: Zhang, C. (2005). Madden–Julian oscillation. Reviews of Geophysics, 43(2).
[^11^]: Madden, R. A., & Julian, P. R. (1971). Detection of a 40–50 day oscillation in the zonal wind in the tropical Pacific. Journal of the Atmospheric Sciences, 28(5), 702-708.
[^12^]: Rostami, M., & Zeitlin, V. (2019). Slow eastward propagation of equatorial modons due to moist convection. Journal of Fluid Mechanics, 874, 174-195.
[^13^]: Rostami, M., & Zeitlin, V. (2020). Generation of MJO-like coherent structures by geostrophic adjustment in the lower troposphere. Journal of the Atmospheric Sciences, 77(7), 2351-2368.
[^14^]: Fovell, R., & Su, H. (2007). Impact of cloud microphysics on convective-radiative quasi equilibrium revealed by cloud-resolving convective parameterization. Journal of the Atmospheric Sciences, 64(11), 4225-4237.

#### Subsection: 10.1d Turbulence and the Indian Ocean Dipole

The Indian Ocean Dipole (IOD), also known as the Subtropical Indian Ocean Dipole (SIOD), is a significant climate phenomenon that demonstrates the impact of turbulence on climate variability. The IOD is characterized by an anomalous cooling or warming of the sea surface temperatures (SST) in the Indian Ocean[^15^]. 

The SIOD has been found to have a significant impact on rainfall patterns in southwestern Australia and southeastern Africa. In southwestern Australia, dry (wet) years correspond to anomalously cool (warm) waters in the tropical/subtropical Indian Ocean and anomalously warm (cool) waters in the subtropics off Australia[^16^]. These anomalies are in phase with the large-scale winds over the tropical/subtropical Indian Ocean, which modify SST anomalies through anomalous Ekman transport in the tropical Indian Ocean and through anomalous air–sea heat fluxes in the subtropics[^16^]. 

In southeastern Africa, positive SIOD events cause increased summer rains by bringing enhanced convergence of moisture. Higher temperature over the Southwestern Indian Ocean warm pole results in increased evaporation, and this moist air is advected to Mozambique and eastern South Africa, which is strengthened by the low pressure anomaly generated over this warm pole[^17^].

Turbulence plays a significant role in the IOD. It can enhance the mixing of heat and momentum in the ocean, potentially amplifying the IOD. On the other hand, turbulence can also dampen the IOD by dispersing energy and heat across a wider area[^18^]. 

The understanding of the IOD and its interaction with turbulence is crucial for accurate weather and climate predictions. Despite significant advancements in our understanding of the IOD, many aspects of its dynamics, structure, and impacts remain poorly understood. Further research is needed to fully understand the role of turbulence in the IOD.

[^15^]: Saji, N.H., Goswami, B.N., Vinayachandran, P.N., & Yamagata, T. (1999). A dipole mode in the tropical Indian Ocean. Nature, 401(6751), 360-363.
[^16^]: Ashok, K., Guan, Z., & Yamagata, T. (2003). Influence of the Indian Ocean Dipole on the Australian winter rainfall. Geophysical Research Letters, 30(15).
[^17^]: Behera, S.K., & Yamagata, T. (2001). Subtropical SST dipole events in the southern Indian Ocean. Geophysical Research Letters, 28(2), 327-330.
[^18^]: Yamagata, T., Behera, S.K., Luo, J.J., Masson, S., Jury, M.R., & Rao, S.A. (2004). Coupled ocean–atmosphere variability in the tropical Indian Ocean. Earth's Climate: The Ocean-Atmosphere Interaction, 147, 189-211.

#### Subsection: 10.2a Turbulence and Global Warming

Global warming, a significant aspect of climate change, is primarily driven by the increase in greenhouse gases in the Earth's atmosphere. This increase in greenhouse gases, particularly carbon dioxide (CO2), traps more heat from the sun, leading to a rise in the Earth's average temperature[^19^]. 

Turbulence plays a crucial role in the distribution and transport of heat and greenhouse gases in the atmosphere and oceans. In the atmosphere, turbulence can enhance the vertical mixing of heat and greenhouse gases, potentially amplifying the effects of global warming[^20^]. In the ocean, turbulence can affect the uptake and storage of heat and CO2, which can have significant implications for global warming[^21^].

Atmospheric and oceanic general circulation models (GCMs) are essential tools for studying the role of turbulence in global warming. These models simulate the interactions of the atmosphere, oceans, land surface, and ice, and can incorporate the effects of turbulence through parameterizations[^22^]. For example, the HadCM3 and EdGCM models include parameterizations for processes such as convection and turbulent mixing that occur on scales too small to be resolved directly[^23^].

However, accurately representing turbulence in GCMs is a significant challenge due to its complex, multiscale nature. Turbulence can occur on scales ranging from millimeters to thousands of kilometers, and can involve a wide range of physical processes[^24^]. Therefore, improving our understanding of turbulence and its representation in GCMs is crucial for improving our predictions of global warming and its impacts.

In addition to GCMs, Earth-system models of intermediate complexity (EMICs) and radiative-convective models (RCMs) can also be used to study the role of turbulence in global warming. For example, the Climber-3 model includes a statistical-dynamical representation of turbulence, while RCMs can be used to verify basic climate assumptions related to turbulence[^25^].

In conclusion, turbulence plays a significant role in global warming, and understanding its effects is crucial for accurate climate predictions. Further research is needed to improve our understanding of turbulence and its representation in climate models.

[^19^]: IPCC, 2014: Climate Change 2014: Synthesis Report. Contribution of Working Groups I, II and III to the Fifth Assessment Report of the Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change [Core Writing Team, R.K. Pachauri and L.A. Meyer (eds.)]. IPCC, Geneva, Switzerland, 151 pp.

[^20^]: Stull, R. B. (1988). An introduction to boundary layer meteorology (Vol. 13). Springer Science & Business Media.

[^21^]: Wunsch, C., & Ferrari, R. (2004). Vertical mixing, energy, and the general circulation of the oceans. Annual review of fluid mechanics, 36, 281-314.

Contribution of Working Group I to the Fourth Assessment Report of the Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change. Cambridge University Press, Cambridge, United Kingdom and New York, NY, USA.

[^23^]: Gordon, C., et al. (2000). The simulation of SST, sea ice extents and ocean heat transports in a version of the Hadley Centre coupled model without flux adjustments. Climate Dynamics, 16(2-3), 147-168.

[^24^]: Pope, S. B. (2000). Turbulent Flows. Cambridge University Press, Cambridge, United Kingdom.

[^25^]: Petoukhov, V., et al. (2000). CLIMBER-2: a climate system model of intermediate complexity. Part I: model description and performance for present climate. Climate Dynamics, 16(1), 1-17.

#### Subsection: 10.2b Turbulence and Sea Level Rise

Sea level rise, another significant aspect of climate change, has been observed globally with an increase of about 20 cm between 1901 and 2018[^25^]. More recent data from satellite radar measurements indicate an acceleration in the rate of sea level rise, with an average increase of 4.62 mm/yr for the decade 2013–2022[^26^].

Turbulence in the ocean and atmosphere plays a critical role in the dynamics of sea level rise. In the ocean, turbulence can influence the distribution and transport of heat and freshwater, which can affect sea level through changes in ocean density and circulation[^27^]. For instance, the warming of the Atlantic Ocean, which is occurring at a faster pace than the Pacific, has led to a sea level rise 3–4 times the global average along the U.S. East Coast and Europe[^28^].

In the atmosphere, turbulence can affect sea level indirectly through its impact on climate variables such as temperature and precipitation. Changes in these variables can alter the rates of glacier melt and ice sheet mass loss, which are major contributors to sea level rise[^29^]. For example, the loss of mass from melting glaciers reduces their gravitational pull, causing local water levels to drop near the glacier while increasing more than average further away[^30^].

General circulation models (GCMs) and Earth-system models of intermediate complexity (EMICs) are valuable tools for studying the role of turbulence in sea level rise. These models can simulate the interactions of the atmosphere, oceans, land surface, and ice, and can incorporate the effects of turbulence through parameterizations[^31^]. However, accurately representing turbulence in these models is a significant challenge due to its complex, multiscale nature[^32^].

In addition to natural processes, human activities such as the extraction of groundwater and oil and gas, as well as flood management practices, can also contribute to sea level rise. These activities can cause land subsidence, leading to a relative increase in sea level in areas built on river deltas[^33^]. Therefore, understanding the interactions between turbulence, climate change, and human activities is crucial for predicting future sea level rise and its impacts on coastal communities and ecosystems.

#### Subsection: 10.2c Turbulence and Ocean Acidification

Ocean acidification, a direct consequence of increased carbon dioxide (CO2) absorption by the oceans, is another critical aspect of climate change. The process begins when CO2 dissolves in seawater, forming carbonic acid and subsequently reducing the pH of the water[^33^]. This phenomenon was observed during the Selli Event, where large-scale volcanic release of CO2 led to a drop in seawater pH, marking the onset of Oceanic Anoxic Event 1a (OAE1a)[^34^].

Turbulence in the ocean plays a significant role in the dynamics of ocean acidification. Turbulence can enhance the mixing and distribution of CO2 in the water column, thereby increasing the rate of acidification[^35^]. This process can have severe implications for marine life, particularly for calcifying organisms such as corals and shellfish, which struggle to build their shells and skeletons in more acidic conditions[^36^].

In the context of OAE1a, turbulence likely played a role in the distribution of the excess CO2 absorbed by the ocean, contributing to the widespread ocean acidification observed during this period[^37^]. This acidification may have been a factor in the significant decline of highly calcified nannoconids, although this causal relationship is disputed[^38^].

Turbulence can also influence the transport and distribution of nutrients in the ocean, which can indirectly affect ocean acidification. For instance, during the Selli Event, increased sulphate flux from volcanism led to an increase in hydrogen sulphide production, which in turn increased phosphorus availability in the water column[^39^]. This increased nutrient availability can stimulate phytoplankton blooms, which absorb CO2 during photosynthesis and can therefore potentially mitigate some effects of ocean acidification[^40^].

However, accurately representing turbulence in models of ocean acidification is a significant challenge due to its complex, multiscale nature[^41^]. General circulation models (GCMs) and Earth-system models of intermediate complexity (EMICs) are valuable tools for studying the role of turbulence in ocean acidification, but they require careful parameterization to accurately incorporate the effects of turbulence[^42^].

In conclusion, turbulence plays a crucial role in the dynamics of ocean acidification, influencing the distribution of CO2 and nutrients in the ocean. Understanding these dynamics is essential for predicting the future impacts of climate change on our oceans and developing effective strategies for mitigation[^43^].

(2012). Climate Change Impacts on Marine Ecosystems. Annual Review of Marine Science, 4, 11-37.

#### Subsection: 10.2d Turbulence and Climate Feedbacks

Turbulence plays a significant role in the climate system, particularly in the context of climate feedbacks. Climate feedbacks are processes that can either amplify (positive feedback) or dampen (negative feedback) the initial climate response to a perturbation[^42^]. Two key climate feedbacks where turbulence plays a crucial role are the water vapor feedback and the cloud feedback.

##### Water Vapor Feedback

The water vapor feedback is a powerful positive feedback in the climate system. As the atmosphere warms due to increased concentrations of greenhouse gases, the saturation vapor pressure increases, leading to an increase in the amount of water vapor in the atmosphere[^43^]. Since water vapor is a potent greenhouse gas, this increase in water vapor content further warms the atmosphere, creating a positive feedback loop[^44^].

Turbulence in the atmosphere can enhance this feedback by increasing the mixing and distribution of water vapor. This process can lead to a more uniform distribution of water vapor in the atmosphere, thereby enhancing the greenhouse effect and further warming the atmosphere[^45^]. However, accurately representing this process in climate models is challenging due to the complex, multiscale nature of turbulence[^46^].

##### Cloud Feedback

Cloud feedback is another critical climate feedback where turbulence plays a significant role. Warming is expected to change the distribution and type of clouds, which can have a significant impact on the Earth's energy balance[^47^]. Low clouds tend to trap more heat at the surface and therefore have a positive feedback, while high clouds normally reflect more sunlight from the top so they have a negative feedback[^48^].

Turbulence can influence the formation, development, and dissipation of clouds. For instance, turbulence can enhance the vertical mixing of air, promoting the formation of low clouds[^49^]. On the other hand, turbulence can also lead to the breakup of clouds, particularly stratocumulus clouds, which can have a significant impact on the Earth's energy balance[^50^].

However, representing the effects of turbulence on clouds in climate models is a significant challenge. The complex, multiscale nature of turbulence makes it difficult to accurately represent in models, leading to uncertainties in the prediction of cloud feedback[^51^]. Despite these challenges, understanding the role of turbulence in climate feedbacks is crucial for improving our ability to predict future climate change[^52^].

[^42^]: Hartmann, D.L., 2015. Global Physical Climatology. Newnes.
[^43^]: Held, I.M., Soden, B.J., 2000. Water vapor feedback and global warming. Annu. Rev. Energy Environ. 25, 441–475.
[^44^]: Sherwood, S.C., et al., 2010. An overview of the water vapor feedback on climate sensitivity. In: The Global Circulation of the Atmosphere. Princeton University Press, pp. 419–449.

#### 10.3a Turbulence and Tropical Cyclones

Tropical cyclones, also known as hurricanes or typhoons, are among the most powerful and destructive weather phenomena on Earth. These storms are characterized by intense low pressure, strong winds, and heavy rainfall, all of which are influenced by turbulence in the atmosphere and ocean[^50^].

Turbulence plays a crucial role in the formation, intensification, and movement of tropical cyclones. The initial formation of a tropical cyclone requires a pre-existing disturbance in the atmosphere, typically a tropical wave. Turbulence within this wave can enhance the vertical mixing of heat and moisture, promoting the development of deep convection and the formation of a cyclone[^51^].

Once a cyclone has formed, turbulence within the storm can contribute to its intensification. The strong winds of a cyclone generate turbulence in the boundary layer, the lowest part of the atmosphere. This turbulence enhances the transfer of heat and moisture from the ocean surface to the atmosphere, fueling the storm[^52^]. Furthermore, turbulence in the upper levels of the storm can influence the distribution of cloud particles and the size of the storm's anvil, as noted by Fovell et al. (2009)[^53^]. This can affect the storm's radiative balance and, consequently, its intensity and track[^54^].

Turbulence can also influence the movement or track of a tropical cyclone. The track of a cyclone is largely determined by the steering flow, the large-scale wind pattern in which the storm is embedded. Turbulence can modify this steering flow, leading to changes in the storm's track[^55^]. Additionally, as F09 noted, storms with stronger outer winds, which are influenced by turbulence, tend to track more to the northwest[^56^].

However, accurately representing the effects of turbulence in tropical cyclone models is a significant challenge. Turbulence is a complex, multiscale process that is not fully understood. Moreover, the effects of turbulence can be amplified or dampened by other factors, such as sea surface temperatures and the storm's internal dynamics[^57^]. Therefore, further research is needed to improve our understanding of turbulence in tropical cyclones and its representation in models.

In the next section, we will explore the role of turbulence in another extreme weather phenomenon: tornadoes.

#### 10.3b Turbulence and Tornadoes

Tornadoes, like tropical cyclones, are significantly influenced by atmospheric turbulence. These violent and destructive weather phenomena are characterized by a rapidly rotating column of air that is in contact with both the surface of the Earth and a cumulonimbus cloud[^57^].

The formation of tornadoes is a complex process that involves several stages, each of which is influenced by turbulence. Initially, a change in wind direction and an increase in wind speed with increasing altitude creates an invisible, horizontal spinning effect in the lower atmosphere. This is known as wind shear[^58^]. Turbulence plays a crucial role in this stage by enhancing the vertical mixing of air masses with different temperatures and humidity levels[^59^].

Next, rising air within the thunderstorm updraft tilts the rotating air from horizontal to vertical, leading to the formation of a tornado[^60^]. Turbulence within the updraft can intensify this rotation and contribute to the development of a tornado[^61^].

Once a tornado has formed, turbulence within the tornado can influence its intensity and path. The strong winds within a tornado generate turbulence, which can enhance the transfer of heat and momentum from the surface to the atmosphere, fueling the tornado[^62^]. Furthermore, turbulence can influence the movement of a tornado. The path of a tornado is largely determined by the steering flow, the large-scale wind pattern in which the tornado is embedded. Turbulence can modify this steering flow, leading to changes in the tornado's path[^63^].

However, accurately representing the effects of turbulence in tornado models is a significant challenge. Turbulence is a complex, multiscale process that is not fully understood. Moreover, the effects of turbulence can vary greatly depending on the specific conditions of the atmosphere and the surface[^64^].

Despite these challenges, understanding the role of turbulence in tornadoes is crucial for improving tornado forecasts and warnings. This can help mitigate the devastating impacts of tornadoes, such as those experienced in the United States in 2017 and 2023[^65^].

[^57^]: NOAA (2021). "What is a tornado?" National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration. Retrieved from https://www.noaa.gov/education/resource-collections/weather-atmosphere-education-resources/tornadoes

#### 10.3c Turbulence and Thunderstorms

Thunderstorms, like tornadoes, are significantly influenced by atmospheric turbulence. These meteorological events are characterized by the presence of thunder, lightning, heavy rain, and sometimes even hail[^65^]. The formation, development, and dissipation of thunderstorms are all processes that are significantly affected by turbulence.

The formation of thunderstorms begins with the lifting of warm, moist air. This can occur due to several mechanisms, such as the heating of the Earth's surface, the presence of a front, or orographic lifting over mountains[^66^]. As this air rises, it cools and condenses to form a cumulonimbus cloud, the characteristic cloud of a thunderstorm[^67^]. Turbulence plays a crucial role in this stage by enhancing the vertical mixing of air masses with different temperatures and humidity levels[^68^].

Once a thunderstorm has formed, turbulence within the storm can influence its development and intensity. The updrafts and downdrafts within a thunderstorm are turbulent, leading to a chaotic and unpredictable flow of air[^69^]. This turbulence can enhance the transfer of heat and momentum within the storm, fueling its growth[^70^]. Furthermore, turbulence can contribute to the formation of severe weather phenomena associated with thunderstorms, such as tornadoes and hail[^71^].

The dissipation of a thunderstorm is also influenced by turbulence. As the storm matures, the downdrafts within the storm become stronger and more turbulent. This turbulence can disrupt the updraft, cutting off the supply of warm, moist air that fuels the storm[^72^]. Eventually, the storm dissipates, leaving behind only a remnant anvil cloud[^73^].

However, accurately representing the effects of turbulence in thunderstorm models is a significant challenge. Turbulence is a complex, multiscale process that is not fully understood. Moreover, the effects of turbulence can vary greatly depending on the specific conditions of the atmosphere and the surface[^74^].

Despite these challenges, understanding the role of turbulence in thunderstorms is crucial for improving weather forecasts and mitigating the impacts of severe weather. Further research is needed to better understand the complex interactions between turbulence and thunderstorms[^75^].

#### 10.3d Turbulence and Extreme Precipitation

Extreme precipitation events, such as heavy rainfalls and snowstorms, are another weather phenomenon significantly influenced by atmospheric turbulence. These events are characterized by high rates of precipitation that can lead to flooding and other severe impacts[^74^].

The formation of extreme precipitation events is closely tied to the presence of moisture in the atmosphere. Moisture-laden air masses can be lifted by various mechanisms, such as frontal systems or orographic lifting, leading to condensation and precipitation[^75^]. Turbulence plays a key role in this process by enhancing the vertical mixing of air masses, which can increase the amount of moisture available for precipitation[^76^].

Turbulence also influences the intensity and duration of extreme precipitation events. The turbulent nature of the atmosphere can lead to the formation of convective cells, which are regions of rising and sinking air that can enhance precipitation[^77^]. Furthermore, turbulence can lead to the formation of mesoscale convective systems, which are large, organized systems of thunderstorms that can produce heavy rainfall over a wide area[^78^].

The dissipation of extreme precipitation events is also influenced by turbulence. As the event progresses, the turbulent mixing of air can lead to the depletion of moisture, reducing the intensity of the precipitation[^79^]. Additionally, turbulence can disrupt the organization of convective systems, leading to their eventual dissipation[^80^].

However, accurately modeling the effects of turbulence on extreme precipitation events is a significant challenge. The multiscale nature of turbulence makes it difficult to represent in numerical weather prediction models, such as the Flow-following, finite-volume Icosahedral Model (FIM) or the Global Forecast System (GFS)[^81^]. Furthermore, the effects of turbulence can vary greatly depending on the specific conditions of the atmosphere, adding another layer of complexity to the problem[^82^].

Despite these challenges, understanding the role of turbulence in extreme precipitation events is crucial for improving our ability to predict these events and mitigate their impacts. Future research in this area will likely involve the development of more sophisticated models that can better represent the complex, multiscale nature of turbulence[^83^].
